Fire safety- and bearing capacity model of super-
structures concerning storey residential construc-
tions from the period 1950-1980
Brandsikrings- og bæreevnemodel ved overbygning på eksisterende etage-
boligbyggeri fra perioden 1950-1980
Marie Rask Jensen, s113800

==PAGE:3==
Summary (English)
The project deals with construction of three leveled superstructures onto existing
apartment buildings speciﬁc for the period of 1950 to 1980 built in Denmark.
Generally, the buildings of choice reﬂects the most common building techniques
used throughout the period.
This thesis provides a thorough analysis of the existing state of the three con-
structions. That is, the three constructions have been analyzed through a reg-
istration based upon the available archive material. Moreover, the registration
is extended by a carrying capacity- and ﬁre analysis. The output is a concise
overview of the residual capacity potential sustained by the respective buildings.
The result of the residual capacity analysis grounds the basis of the proposed
design solutions. Providing, in each case, a three leveled superstructure on the
existing three-storey house. In addition, the superstructures are executed using
a wood module construction, aligned with Scandi Byg’s construction processes.
This solution minimizes inconveniences towards the existing residents, which is
one of the goals of this project.
Speciﬁcally the outcome of this project is a preliminary investigation along with
a construction proposal of three diﬀerent three leveled superstructures, provid-
ing:
• Stability analysis of the three existing constructions.
• Residual capacity analysis of the three existing constructions.
• Fire analysis of the existing constructions.
==PAGE:4==
ii
Summary (English)
• Carrying capacity calculations of the wood modules, used in the super-
structures.
• Carrying capacity calculations of the force transfer construction, between
the new and existing building components.
• The existing bearing structures are controlled with respect to the greater
weight provided by the superstructures.
• Thorough ﬁre analysis of the entire building after the expansion.
The carrying capacity calculations provides an analysis of the supporting struc-
tures of both the superstructures as well as the existing construction. Addition-
ally, the constructions are secured for ﬁre performance of applicable require-
ments, with a ﬁre strategy of the new properties, where the existing building
plus the superstructure is analyzed as a single construction.
In conclusion, simple 3D models for the designed superstructures, of the three
investigated houses, have been prepared. In addition, three diﬀerent methods for
connecting an elevator to the existing building are illustrated. The 3D models
oﬀers a visual overview of all of the above aspects, which are highlighted in the
project, and how they play together as one entity.
==PAGE:5==
Summary (Danish)
Målet for denne afhandling er at undersøge tre konkrete etageboligbyggerier fra
perioden 1950-1980 i Danmark. Bygningerne repræsenterer de mest anvendte
forskellige bygningsmetoder for perioden.
Afhandlingen indeholder en grundig analyse af de eksisterende forhold for tre
ejendomme. Ejendommene er analyseret gennem registrering baseret på tilgæn-
gelig eksisterende arkivmateriale.
Ud fra registreringen er der udarbejdet en kontrol af bæreevne forlænget af en
brandanalyse for de eksisterende forhold. Udfaldet heraf er konkretiseret i et
overblik over eventuel restbæreevne af de eksisterende forhold for de respektive
tre ejendomme.
Analysen af restbæreevne skaber grundlag for design udfaldet af overbygninger-
ne. Her er der tale om en tre etagers overbygning for hver af de tre eksisterende
tre-etagers ejendomme. Overbygningerne er konstrueret af træ-modulbyggeri ba-
seret på Scandi Byg’s byggeprincipper. Ved benyttelse af modulbyggeri mind-
skes desuden gener for de eksisterende beboere, der er et af hovedmålene for
udformningen af overbygningen.
Produktet af dette projekt indeholder de indledende undersøgelser, samt et pro-
duktforslag til konstruering af tre forskellige tre-etagers overbygninger, beståen-
de af:
• Stabilitetsanalyse af de tre eksisterende konstruktioner.
==PAGE:6==
iv
• Restbæreevneanalyse af de eksisterende forhold for de tre ejendomme.
• Brandanalyse for de eksisterende forhold for de undersøgte ejendomme.
• Bæreevneberegninger af træmoduler anvendt i overbygningen.
• Bæreevneanalyse for overførelsessystem mellem eksisterende konstruktio-
ner og konstruktioner i overbygningen.
• De eksisterende konstruktioner tjekkes for den øgede lastpåvirkning fra
overbygningen.
• Gennemgående brandanalyse for bygningerne efter udførelse af overbyg-
ningen.
Bæreevneberegningerne analyserer de bærende elementer i både overbygninger-
ne og de eksisterende bebyggelser. I tilknytning til bæreevneberegningerne, er
de bærende konstruktioner i såvel overbygningen som i det eksisterende byggeri
blevet gennemregnet og sikret for opfyldelse af gældende brandkrav. Hertil fo-
religger en brandstrategi for den samlede nye ejendom, hvor eksisterende samt
overbygningen er analyseret som én samlet konstruktion.
Afslutningsvis er der udarbejdet simple 3D-modeller for de valgte overbygninger.
Hertil er der ligeledes illustreret tre forskellige metoder for kobling af elevator
til det eksisterende byggeri. 3D-modellerne giver et godt overblik over alle de
ovenstående aspekter, der er belyst i projektet og hvordan de spiller sammen
som en helhed.

Chapter 1
Introduction
In the following chapter, the issues of this thesis will be introduced. In speciﬁc,
the chapter clariﬁes why this subject is important to elucidate. Likewise, the
working method and process will be reviewed.
1.1
Problem deﬁnition
Renovation work covers many diﬀerent issues and opportunities. Building styles
and material usage have changed over time, as well as the optimization of the
constructions bearing systems. Thus, diﬀerent issues and possibilities will be
encountered depending on the time period at hand. In this thesis, it has been
decided to look into constructions built in Denmark from the period 1950-1980.
Renovation requires an analytical and computational review of the existing
building to provide a clear picture of its complexity. Likewise, it is important
to consider what methods and techniques, that was used during the period. In
order to illuminate this area and create a functional perspective of the build-
ing habits throughout the period, the analysis will focus on three individual
three-storey buildings located around the country.
In particular, the analysis will carry out calculations of the bearing structures
==PAGE:14==
2
Introduction
in the buildings, and the residual capacity will be documented. In addition,
there will be a review of existing ﬁre measures as well as a review of their ﬁt in
modern ﬁre requirements.
The purpose of the above described analysis is to compile a theoretical building
model for the period. The aim of the model is the possibility of expanding the
existing construction vertically. Therefore, the three analyzed properties repre-
sents three diﬀerent construction practices, that were used during the period.
Based on analysis of each case study, this thesis provides a solution consisting
of a three leveled superstructure build upon the existing constructions. This is
followed by an upgrade of the ﬁre safety of the properties, ensuring that both the
existing and new construction complies with current ﬁre requirements. Likewise,
the diﬀerent possibilities to incorporate elevators to the existing buildings will
be reﬂected, as well as utilization of them in relation to ﬁre.
To make a delimitation, no calculations will be made in relation to the energy
perspectives of the properties. Similarly, no detailed design proposals are made
for the superstructures, however an overall design solution will be illustrated.
The focus points of this dissertation are thus carrying capacity and ﬁre condi-
tions.
1.2
Motivation
351.936 apartment buildings was constructed throughout Denmark during the
period of 1950 to 1980, accounting for an entire 17 percent of all apartment
buildings existing in Denmark [sta17]. The big cities in Denmark are growing
vastly, demanding a need of aﬀordable urban apartments. Since many of these
buildings are already present in the dense city environment, it makes sense to
explore the opportunities of extending these constructions with modern super-
structures.
Renovation is exciting as it deals with the preservation of everyone’s history.
There are diﬀerent positions on older constructions versus new, however in spite
of various opinions one cannot ignore existing buildings, and their inﬂuence on
the building styles throughout the country.
Teaching of building habits in relation to older buildings, are limited or op-
tional. Whereas methods, materials, and the techniques of modern buildings are
required. Learning about today’s building style and requirements creates the
basis for working with renovation. But knowledge of old drawings and building
==PAGE:15==
1.3 Related work and Contribution
3
habits, from the time of construction, is also a necessity. A renovation project
is not just about how building requirements are met, but it also aﬀects how to
preserve, reuse, and reinforce the existing building in the best way possible.
The market potential within renovation for engineers is large.
Most of the
properties in inner Copenhagen consist of constructions from the period 1850-
1950, but areas around Copenhagen and similar cities consist mainly of buildings
from the following period of 1950-1980, which is the period to be considered in
this thesis. The interesting part of this period is, that it represent a of transition
period from traditional constructions to newer constructions consisting mainly
of concrete in terms of insitu or elements. The traditional construction methods
has been used throughout decades, where it has been optimized and improved.
Contrary the new construction methods have been less explored and are not yet
fully optimized. Thus, while focusing on a relatively short time period, it still
contains signiﬁcantly diﬀerent building concepts within the period, which are
interesting to compare and investigate for their various possibilities.
Similarly, constructions from this period are characterized by the fact, that
they are not always built in ﬁve to six ﬂoors, lower constructions are often used
spanning only three to four ﬂoors. During renovation of constructions built in
the previous period 1850-1950, utilization of the attics are often investigated.
However, the investigated period in this thesis 1950-1980 opens up the possibility
of adding superstructures of several ﬂoors. Thus existing buildings can be used
for urban renewal and house more people in the light of growing population.
1.3
Related work and Contribution
Traditional masonry techniques used for multileveled apartment constructions
have already been subject to analysis regarding the creation of superstructures.
R. Jensen [Jen15b] cataloged the residual bearing capacity of typical masonry
buildings built during 1850 to 1950. The thesis concludes that the main par-
tition walls are severely overloaded, however that residual capacity is found in
the facades. It is established, that using the masonry buildings for multileveled
superstructures are unfeasible, but inclusion of the attics is possible. The over-
loaded partition walls in masonry buildings are supported by B. Clausen et
al. [Ing]. Moreover, M. Therese et al. [Hal16] provides a thorough investigation
of the multilevel superstructure potential, of masonry apartment buildings built
in 1850-1950.
Their research indicates that special designed superstructures
might be possible to some extent. However, they struggle towards ﬁnding the
complete residual capacity needed. Thus, further research is needed.
==PAGE:16==
4
Introduction
As the preceding research has shown masonry apartment buildings, from the
period of 1850 to 1950, sustain great potential for attic inclusion. However,
multileveled superstructures are more complex. This thesis investigates typical
apartment buildings from the following period of 1950 to 1980. Whilst the pre-
vious period mainly consisted of masonry constructions, this period introduced
concrete techniques. For this reason, this time range is very interesting. First
of all, it makes sense to verify if the masonry buildings of this period follows
the same pattern as the period before. Secondly, as this period introduced con-
crete building techniques, it makes sense to extend the research domain to these
construction types as well. Additionally, this thesis investigates the options for
advancing the existing construction with elevators. It is of high interest to look
at elevator placement, as a standard for this has yet to be evolved [Dam06].
Moreover, as this thesis deals with multileveled buildings spanning more than
three plans, it is required to establish elevators [BR115].
This thesis, provides two actual superstructure proposals, designed and dimen-
sioned using wooden modular blocks, underlying the potential of this techniques.
A. Beim, S. Bjarløv et al. [AB11] compiled an investigation of modular construc-
tion techniques. This article concluded, that the use of wood modular blocks can
be economically beneﬁcial, without compromising the architectural, technical,
or residential satisfaction.
Fire safety in multileveled apartment buildings have become of great interest.
It is public known, that ﬁre safety in apartment buildings is of high importance,
[Chr13] [Ves13].
Naturally, this is of high importance since the human risk
is very high in these areas. This research, does not only analyze the existing
conditions of apartment constructions from this period, it concludes a strategy
for updating the ﬁre safety to modern conditions.
From the discussion follows the main contributions of this research, all regarding
three leveled apartment buildings built in Denmark from 1950 to 1980:
• Extensive literature study comparing the regulations from the period 1950
to 1980 with modern requirements, with focus on ﬁre.
• Registration of the existing constructions, based on archive material and
on site visual inspections.
• Residual capacity investigation of masonry apartment buildings.
• Residual capacity investigation of concrete based apartment buildings.
• Fire safety analysis of the existing apartment buildings.
==PAGE:17==
1.4 Metods & process
5
• Fire safety analysis plus upgrading to comply with modern requirements.
(Post superstructure)
• Analysis of elevator placement in apartment buildings including the new
superstructures. (Post superstructure)
• Dimensioning of wood modular based three leveled superstructures on
existing apartment buildings.
• Multiple apartment layout proposals focusing on designing for families,
students, and disabled people.
• 3D-design proposals of three leveled superstructures on existing apartment
buildings.
1.4
Metods & process
Under the development of this thesis, diﬀerent methods are used that together
will shape the project process. The methods are based on the various steps
through the report, each of which are shaping the basis for the method and
process of the project.
1 Literature study, background studies
First and foremost, the background knowledge concerning the con-
struction and ﬁre laws is acquired for the period. Based on a lit-
erature study consisting of articles, old building laws, etc.
This
background check will be used to provide the necessary knowledge
required to produce realistic refurbishment of the buildings. In addi-
tion, this knowledge is necessary to provide an understanding of- and
a better insight into the technical solutions used during the period.
2 Further analysis, cases studies
With the knowledge from the above method, three diﬀerent cases are
found to represent the period. For each case, material are found in the
associated municipality archives and an exterior view is performed as
supplement to the archive material, to give a better understanding
of the drawing material. All dimensions used in the report are based
on the archive material. Related carrying capacity calculations of
existing conditions are based on the current Danske Standarter. In
continuation of the carrying capacity calculations, a ﬁre analysis is
performed for the existing conditions, based on the current legisla-
tion from the time of construction. The aim of this part, where the
==PAGE:18==
6
Introduction
three cases are investigated, is to create a holistic impression of the
structure and its development during the period.
3 Solution options, concept development
In this method diﬀerent solutions for the superstructures will be pre-
sented and compared.
Based on the results, of the two methods
above, the amount of useful residual capacity of the buildings can be
determined. This knowledge is used to ﬁnd the most optimal solution
for the superstructures. In addition, the impact of the superstructure
on the ﬁre safety is presented.
4 Solution, superstructures
Based on all of the above methods, the ﬁnal solution proposals are
prepared. In addition, carrying capacity calculations are made for
existing as well as the new constructions. Similarly, ﬁre strategies
are prepared for the evacuation and construction of the renovated
buildings. All this ends in 3D models, illustrating the three renovated
properties, with accompanying plan solutions, to visualize the overall
impression.
The project structure is created based on the above 4 methods, which together
form the process of the project. We start broadly to look at the background
for the period.
This knowledge is then made concrete by focusing on three
buildings from the period. Then the project is narrowed once more by focusing
on the residual capacity in the building, for the choice of optimal solution of the
superstructures. Finally, all overheads end up in the development of 3 solutions
for the superstructures, the structure is illustrated in Figure 1.1.

Outline
Chapter 2-Background studies
This chapter provides the reader with the building technology and
ﬁre requirements regarding the investigated period 1950-1980. This
information will be used throughout this thesis.

Based on three cases this chapter will present information regarding
diﬀerent types of buildings in the period. The analyses will consist
of registration, bearing capacity control and ﬁre safety control for all
three cases.

The potential for superstructures will now be determined based on
the results found in the privies chapter. Diﬀerent solutions will be
presented and the most optimal related to the investigated studies
will be determined.

A static veriﬁcation of the superstructures are documented along with
the most relevant detailed connections.

This chapter includes a ﬁre strategy for the three cases, both existing
and new building parts. The ﬁre strategy concerns both pre-ﬂashover
and post-ﬂashover ﬁre safety.

The visual design of the superstructures are presented for all cases.
Likewise, diﬀerent types of apartment decor is shown.
Chapter 8-Discussion
The contribution of this thesis becomes subject to discussion. That is,
this chapter will look at all the diﬀerent aspects there are highlighted
through the thesis.
Chapter 9-Conclusion
The project is rounded up, and future steps are discussed.
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2
Literature studies
To make a scope of the thesis a background study has been made. This part is
base on a literature survey, which includes everything from old articles to old
laws and regulations. It is impotant to emphasize that this is not meant to be a
detail description, but more an introduction and outline for the building methods
and ﬁre regulations/laws regarding the case studies in this thesis. All ﬁre safety
regarding air-raid shelter will be left out.
2.1
Building methods
The following section is primarily based on the references; Register on Danish
building style [Eng17] [oR17], books about older storey buildings construction
and renovation [Eng13] [Eng83] [oJm87] [Bon71] [Gra ] [Jen15b] and handbook
for building industry, material situation and construction [AWH42]
Building standards, laws, and traditions are changed and/or replaced over time.
However, some persists for many years. For this reason, it is of high importance
not only to look at the concerned period between 1950-1980, but also the pre-
ceding time. In fact, sometimes when a law is changed, the new law may still
refer to previous laws. Thus, it is crucial to know the past as it grounds the basis
of future laws. For this reason, the following sections will brieﬂy document the
==PAGE:22==
10
Literature studies
period ranging from 1850 to 1950, if needed a detailed description can be found
in [Jen15b], providing a thorough documentation of building habits during 1850
to 1950. Typical building models throughout the period can be inspected in
Figure 2.1.
During 1850-1890 there was one type of apartment houses which was largely
unique for that period. This construction consisted of: endemic massive brick
facade walls; foundation in tile; pitched roof with tiles, slates, or Copenhagen
roof with zinc; inner walls of timber-framing; horizontal divisions of wood tier
beams; and staircases of wood.
Throughout the consecutive span of 1890-1920 the use of concrete gradually
began to make its mark on the construction industry.
The foundation and
basement walls were cast on site in concrete. Mansard-roof began to emerge as
the preferred roof construction. Moreover, the main partition wall and staircase
walls were switched from timber-framing to massive brick walls. The horizontal
divisions started to obtain iron beams by eg. bays.
In 1920-1940 a new construction type initiated new standards, which ranged
until the material shortage of iron arose due to World War II. This type fea-
tured greater balcony impact since they could replace the back staircase. The
increasing use of balconies, along with the growing appearance of corner win-
dows yielded a high demand for iron in the construction. More ﬂeet roofs started
to appear with cement-based plates and rooﬁng paper. Inner walls began to be
a mixture between masonry and double brick walls, while the ﬂoor partitions
were made of wood tier beams with a predominantly part of iron beams.
By the end of the 1940s, some signiﬁcant changes emerged within Danish con-
struction practices, which formed the basis of the common 1950 buildings. In
the period between 1940-1960 the massive brick walls were gradually replaced
by hollow brick walls with ﬁrm binders.
Especially the gable walls and the
top ﬂoor walls were changed. Also it became more the rule than the avoid-
ance that the inner walls were of masonry. The horizontal divisions were now
made of diﬀerent concrete and tilt designs. Typically reinforced concrete slabs
cast on site or diﬀerent types of hollow-decks. Furthermore, this type of houses
contained only one staircase made of concrete with prefabricated landings. Ar-
chitecturally the biggest change in these houses were the inaugural balconies
that gained considerable ground.
Apartment houses from 1960 and forward diﬀers from all the previous types.
All bearing construction parts now consisted only of concrete. The foundation
and basement walls were still always cast on site.
However, the remaining
building components could consist of either prefabricated concrete elements or
cast on site. The horizontal divisions now began to stretch between the stiﬀening
==PAGE:23==
2.1 Building methods
11
transverse walls instead of the main partition wall as previously. In addition,
the facades were no longer part of the bearing construction. Thus, they could
be made of both light- or heavyweight structures.
The carrying gable walls
are typically constructed of sandwich elements, but can also be found as facing
walls. It is the gable and transitional walls that are the stabilizing walls of the
construction.
(a) Type 1: 1850-1890
(b) Type 2: 1890-1920
(c) Type 3: 1920-1940
(d) Type 4: 1940-1960
(e) Type 5: 1960 and forward
Figure 2.1: Building models used for apartment buildings in Denmark.
Around the 1950s, the work balance between the architect and the engineer be-
gan to change. Previously, it was mainly the architect who were responsible for
residential buildings, but this changed in the 1950s. Before 1950, the buildings
where mainly based on laws, load-bearing tables as well as instructions from
the manufacturer. However, after 1950 a lot of development in the building
industry emerged and the elementary building method started to put its mark
on the industry. This new type of construction required a major initiative by
the engineers. Similarly, the conversion from bearing brick stone facades and
main partition-walls to bearing concrete cross-walls and gable walls inﬂuenced
the entire static system of the structures. Hence, it was necessary to document
the bearing capacity and the stability of the new construction model dealing
==PAGE:24==
12
Literature studies
with concrete elements or concrete cast on site.
LN-BO
The following section is primarily based on an article from the magazine "Arkitek-
ten" from 1963 [Ark63] and a journal about the development of building tech-
nology in Denmark after World War II [Sko ].
In the 1960s there were an increase in housing shortage, which was caused by
the population growth and big settlement in the cities. This resulted in a need
for cheap housing.
Legislative and admitting instances began to look at the possibilities of ra-
tionalization and industrialization of constructions, in the hope of a cheaper
construction industry. However, this insight into the construction was not done
without any protests from the craftsmen. On the other hand, it opened the
doors for standard houses and components. However, industrialization of the
construction industry is not just a change, that can be made from one day to
the other. It requires greater investment in the production equipment than that
of traditional constructions, which means demand for greater sales stability. In
addition, concerns were raised by architects against type houses and mass pro-
duction. However, this did not stop the development and type housing to be
realised.
LN-BO was the ﬁrst and largest real type of a standard storey-construction. The
type house was produced by the pioneering business Larsen & Nielsen, L&N,
in 1962. L&N sold licenses to many diﬀerent countries over the following years,
including Sweden, Germany, England, Malaysia, and Czeko-Slovakia. License
sales occurred for two main reasons: Economy and a common housing type
across countries. Developing a common housing type across countries, did how-
ever not succeed because of diﬀerences in the building regulations and traditions
across the diﬀerent countries.
The standard houses initially consisted of three storey buildings with the loca-
tions in; Rødovre, Espergærde, Odense, Mariager, Slagelse, Holbæk, and Hels-
ingør among others. The market was almost exclusively on Zealand, since the
element factories were located on Zealand. Later on, a factory was made in Jut-
land. The company had capacity for approximately 1400 apartments per year.
Which corresponds to approx. 5% of the construction industry in the country
at that time. That was not enough to make a decisive imprint in the landscapes
appearance, and also not enough to "overthrow" the traditional construction
industry. That said, LN-BO became a successful building style through the 60s
==PAGE:25==
2.1 Building methods
13
and 70s. The concept gave freedom to both the developer and the architect in
choosing of eg. plan solutions. The ﬂexible building system was extended and
now it included solutions for three to eight storey buildings.
This ﬂexible building principal initiated interest of standard housing among the
architects. Since it was suﬃciently ﬂexible to provide an architecturally beau-
tiful result. Signiﬁcant for the period is the well proportioned building lengths
combined with a number of ﬂoors to create strong horizontal lines. Similarly, the
architect began to look at the combination of several buildings on the same site
with diﬀerent sizes. Thus instead of only focusing on the individual house, the
domain was increased to a larger area with more buildings. Hence the typical ar-
chitectural view included: How the area appears in sets, and how constructions
inﬂuences each other, typically with respect towards sharing sunlight between
the buildings.
It was not only the ﬂexible options, that made LN-BO get a stable impression
in the construction industry, other factors had a great impact as well:
• Fixed Price
• Fixed delivery
• Fast and easy dimensioning based on catalogs
• Large modules / components
The large components gave a reduced need for transportation and on site pro-
cessing. Approximately 50% of the work was already been done at the factory.
The wall and facade elements were room sized and door/window frames and
electrical pipes were cast in the concrete at the factory. In addition, the win-
dow glass was also installed at the factory, which made it possible to close the
building quickly, making the system ideal during winter as well. In addition,
the bathrooms were delivered as closed units ready to mount. The bathroom
unit is illustrated on Figure 2.2.
==PAGE:26==
14
Literature studies
Figure 2.2: LN-BO bathroom element, [Ark63].
In general, the industrialization provided several beneﬁts to the construction
marked. However, the automization in construction fabrics had a natural limit
in Denmark. Since Denmark has a relatively small construction market, the
high level of investment required is not feasible. The reason why this type of
housing did not have a greater impact on the market was due to the fact that the
economic gain was not suﬃciently large and could not be directly documented.
This is because the price negotiations in social building cases were drowned
by the Ministry of Housing, which sets the price for the developer. Therefore,
each time L&N started a new case, they would have to document price changes
relative to the previous case, otherwise the developer would get the same price
again. Even-though, the economic gain was not conspicuous, there were social
beneﬁts that could not be overlooked. Societal advantages such as:
• Unskilled jobs at the element factories.
• Quick production and completion.
• Faster fulﬁllment of housing needs.
• Save of building loan rates for the developer (rapid production
rate).
==PAGE:27==
2.2 Fire regulations
15
2.2
Fire regulations
The focus towards ﬁre safety emerged a long time before 1950, that is before
the time-span of this report. In fact, severe attention was brought towards the
subject already in the late eightteen-hundred after Copenhagen was ravaged by
two large ﬁres in 1728 and 1795. The devastating eﬀects of these ﬁres became
a realization of the necessity of greater ﬁre regulations. It was at this point
the ﬁrst regulations and laws concerning ﬁre safety, that developed and more
followed in the years to come.
Fire legislation is in constant change given that building standards change over
time. Even though ﬁre legislation was an upcoming topic already in the 18th
century, the regulations are still not clear in the period of 1950-1980. There-
for, a large part of the decisions were handed over to the estimations of local
authorities, where practical experience played a major role.
Fire regulations and laws was denoted using a special tag system consisting of
up to four parts, available from Equation 2.1 and Table 2.1.
⟨resistance class⟩−⟨object⟩−⟨resistance time⟩−⟨covering class⟩

==PAGE:29==
2.2 Fire regulations
17
Fire legislation is a very comprehensive and complex topic, because it contains
many diﬀerent types of laws and regulations from diﬀerent ministries.
The
ministries associated with ﬁre safety are brieﬂy presented below [fh72].
Ministry of Housing
• Building regulations, exact ﬁre requirements
• Controlled by the Building Inspector
Ministry of Justice
• Fire service laws
• Controlled by the Building Inspector
Ministry of Labour
• Laws for industrial safety
• Controlled by the working environment inspector
Interior Ministry
• Structural civil defence measures.
• Controlled by the building inspector
As presented above, there is a broad range of diﬀerent laws and regulations,
which are enforced by several ministries.
The most essential ﬁre legislation,
regarding the following case studies, is shortly presented below. That is, only
the paragraphs and items with importance towards the case studies will be
listed.
Law on structural civil defence measures in 1950 [byg17]
da-dk: Lov om bygningsmæssige civilforanstaltninger, 27. maj 1950.
• This law focuses on the story partitions. The deck above the base-
ment and the roof deck must both be ﬁreproof to prevent spread of
ﬁre and lay on ﬁreproofed constructions.
Building regulations in 1961 [Bol17]
da-dk: Bygningsreglement for Købstæderne og landet, 1. marts 1961.
• The building regulation provides a more overall insight into ﬁre safety
with speciﬁc claims, but without examples and explanations to com-
ply them. Relevant requirements are listed below:
- There must be a rescue opening in all apartments on each ﬂoor
==PAGE:30==
18
Literature studies
- The staircase must have a minimum width of 1 meter and have at
least 2 meters free hight. Furthermore, the staircase must have ﬁre-
proof walls and ﬂame retardant ceilings and a smoke hatch of 1 m2.
- The ﬁre walls must extend from basement to roof, with maximum
holes of 1/3 of the total length, with maximum size of 1 × 2.2 m2
closed with at least a BSD60 door.
Law on structural civil defence measures in 1962 [byg17]
da-dk: Lov om bygningsmæssige civilforanstaltninger, 16 april. 1962
• This law repeals the corresponding law from 1950. Overall there is no
big diﬀerence, although this edition is more detailed. One addition
is the regulations regarding ﬁre protection of iron.
Building regulations in 1966 [Bol17]
da-dk: Bygningsreglement for Købstæderne og landet, 1. August 1966
• Similar to the law on structural civil defence measures, there is not
a big diﬀerence to the 1960 regulations. Once again it is more de-
tailed than previously, which is indicated by a bigger use of the ﬁre
designations, which deﬁnes precise requirements to the diﬀerent con-
structions. Some relevant examples are given below;
- The distance between buildings must be minimum 2.5 m from neigh-
boring boundaries.
- Staircase constructions must be at least BS30 and apartment doors
BD-door30.
- Doors to basement or attic must be BD-door60 or F-door90.
- Fire cells size must be maximum 150 m2 and with BS60 constructions.
Furthermore, ﬂat division walls must be minimum BS60, F-90 or
BD90 and other partitions BD30.
Executive notice on structures as ﬁreproof story partitions in 1969 [byg17]
da-dk: Bekendtgørelse om godkendelse af konstruktioner som brandsikre
etageadskillelser, 18. august 1969
• This notice contains details regarding the civil law in 1962. It ex-
plains what are meant by the diﬀerent ﬁre designations and how to
fulﬁll them. It establishes that by a ﬁreproof construction it means
a construction of minimum BS60.
Guidance on ﬁre protection of building constructions 1972 [byg17]
da-dk: Vejledning om brandsikring af bygningskonstruktioner, 1 juli 1972
==PAGE:31==
2.2 Fire regulations
19
• The civil defence measures, from this time on wards, will be a part of
the building regulations. One of the changes is that the requirement
of ﬁreproof constructions above top ﬂoor is annulled. A new focus is
the ensemble between the diﬀerent building parts in order to maintain
overall stability and bearing capacity in case of ﬁre. An example of
the new guidance can be seen on Figure 2.3.

Building regulations in 1972 [Bol17]
da-dk: Bygningsreglement 1. Juni 1972
• This regulations is more accurate than the previous editions, but also
with a few additions listed below:
- Some speciﬁc claims for stair design are set; the ground must be 25
cm with a rise of max. 18 cm, however in the basement a ground of
21 cm and a rise below is acceptable.
- The smoke hatch in the staircase must be able to be operated from
the ground level.
- Maximum size for a ﬁre section is set to 600 m2.
- Speciﬁc requirements for the wall covering: On all walls in common
areas Class 1 covering is demanded, for the remaining walls Class 2
is acceptable. Furthermore, all ceilings must be covered by Class 1
material.

Fire protection of older buildings in 1976 [Bol76]
da-dk: Brandsikring af ældre ejendomme, Boligministeriet marts 1976
• This law collection corresponds to today’s sample collection (Eksem-

amples to fulﬁll the diﬀerent requirements. To give an example of the
level of detail in the collection, the requirements for rescue openings
and rescue area is presented:
- There are diﬀerent ways to perform a rescue opening. If a side window
is used it must have a min. size of 90 cm×40 cm, in relation a tilt
window requires a minimum size of 55 cm×90 cm. A tilt window can
also be used if the dimensions are 65 cm×80 cm as long as the total
is min. 145 cm and non of the sides are below 55 cm.
- Another important requirement which can be diﬃcult to ﬁgure out
is the claim for a rescue area for the ﬁre service. The area must be
accessible and free for cars, waste containers etc. at any time. The
size of the required area is diﬀerent from ﬁre service to ﬁre service,
because the ﬁre service operates diﬀerent cranes. On Figure 2.4 the
most common areas are shown.

Chapter 3
Case studies
In this chapter, three characteristic apartment buildings from the period 1950-
1980 will be presented by a short registry and carrying capacity calculations, in
order to document the residual capacity of the existing buildings.
3.1
Introduction
It has been chosen to focus on three buildings from the period 1950-1980 located
throughout Denmark.
All buildings are constructed with three ﬂoors and a
basement. The houses are determined on the basis of Chapter 2, in which the
most classic building methods of the period are brieﬂy described. The reasoned
behind the choice of buildings has been to represent the various building trends
from this period, which are:
1 Transition from traditional masonry buildings to prefabricatied
2 Usage of insitu cast concrete
3 Introduction of concrete elements
4 Creation of standard housing
==PAGE:36==
24
Case studies
The load-bearing parts of the building range from masonry, hollow deck, and
concrete in the form of insitu or prefabricated elements. The registration of
the diﬀerent buildings will mainly be based on the obtained archive material.
However, they will be accompanied with an exterior view of the building, in
order to create a better overview of the drawing material. This means that
registrations and calculations are based on initial conditions. Thus, potential
modiﬁcations over time are neglected, unless they are suﬃciently documented
in the archive material.
In addition, all calculations are performed based on measurements from the
original drawings, as no measurements are made during this survey. Calculations
regarding staircases, balconies, bays, installations, lintel at windows and doors
will not be made. Calculations are made on a appropriate section of the building,
in relation to the design structure.
The calculations are to be used for the
renovation of the building.

3.2
Load combinations
There are a great number of diﬀerent load combinations for both Ultimate Limit
State (ULS) and Serviceability Limit State (SLS). This thesis will not be inves-
tigating any SLS load combinations, but only ULS load combinations.
The relevant load combinations varies for each construction. When initiating a
project, it is thus a good idea to look at the diﬀerent load combinations, and
then use the exclusion method to ﬁnd the most critical ones. A brief analysis
==PAGE:37==
3.2 Load combinations
25
of the most common load combinations, which will be used in this thesis, are
listed below.
Primary ﬂoor: Since all ﬂoors have the same design and usage, it is not rele-
vant to look at load combinations for diﬀerent primary ﬂoors.
Dominant load: The four main load groups (permanent, pay, snow, and wind)
can all operate as dominant load, but it is rarely necessary to decide all
of them.
- For the existing construction the roof structures are not checked,
due to the fact that there conditions will change doing a renovation.
Dominant snow load is therefor not relevant for these calculations.
On the other hand it could be relevant for the new building due to
the use of ﬂat roof.
- In terms of dominant payload and permanent load, payloads will be
relevant in the upper ﬂoors and permanent loads in the lower ﬂoors
as the percentage of permanent load increases.
- Wind load is especially interesting for the building after the added
superstructure is build, as the building gets twice as high.
Favour and unfavorable: In addition, the diﬀerent load groups can act in
favour or as an disadvantage for the construction. This is of particular
interest when the stability of the structures are investigated.
Based on this short presentation, it has been decided to look at four diﬀer-
ent load combinations throughout this thesis. It is estimated that these will
be the determining parameters of most relevance regarding this investigation;
when taking into account both the calculations of existing conditions and new
conditions:
STR1 Dominant live load
STR2 Dominant dead load
STR3 Dominant wind (unfavorable)
STR4 Dominant wind (favour)
The diﬀerent load combinations with associated factors are illustrated in Table
3.2. The factors are determined by [mf.16].

House 1, traditional building
The property is from 1956 and located in Hjørring, Northen Jutland. Originally
the building was built as a stand alone construction, however a later connection

and location of staircases.

Main structure
The building geometry is a rectangular block with 6 entrances. The basement is
used for bicycles, prams, air-raid shelter, etc. The rest of the ﬂoors are used for
residential purpose. The building is built upon load-bearing facades and a main
partition wall, in which the loads are led down to the foundation. The design
of the apartments are the same through the entire building with the exception
of both ends, where it diﬀers slightly, see Figure 3.3. There are two bearing
partition walls located at the stairwells and otherwise only one main partition
wall.
(a) Floor level
==PAGE:41==

29
(b) Basement level
Figure 3.3: House 1: Basement and ﬂoor level layout.
Roof
The roof of the building is a pitched roof. The connection between the roof
and bearing walls diﬀers depending on whether it is at the stairways or not,
see Figure 3.4. At the stairways the two bearing partition walls continues all
the way to the roof. For the remaining roof part there is a single truss in the
roof structure, which transfers the load from the roof structure, so that both
the facades and the main partition wall absorbs the load. The most signiﬁcant
height dimensions can be read on Figure 3.4.
The roof cover is made from
eternit-slate with the size 30 cm X 60 cm on both sides.
Figure 3.4: House 1: Elevation drawing with and without the staircase, to
illustrate the continuous walls and the roof layout.
==PAGE:42==
30
Case studies
Horizontal division
The horizontal divisions consists of 10 cm concrete deck above the basement.
In the remaining ﬂoors the divisions are made by 15 cm Bauma-deck. The roof
deck is likewise 15 cm Bauma [51]. Finally, the basement deck above the air-raid
shelter is 20 cm concrete.
Hollow block deck can be made using many diﬀerent types of deck designs,
which is partially fabricated.
Hollow block deck constitute the transition to
elementary building. It is a reinforced concrete deck with embed blocks. A
hollow block deck is usually higher than reinforced concrete deck, but with a
smaller weight and less iron and cement consumption. The blocks are laid in
rows with a tight underside and the grooves on the upper size are provided with
reinforcement and cast with concrete.
Economically, it is better to increase
the block height rather than applying stronger reinforcement. The same block
height is therefore used for the entire deck and any variation of bearing capacity
occurs in the reinforcement.
The construction begins at the facades. Every other row starts with a half block
such that the rows are shifted. The blocks have the dimension of 25 cm x 25
cm. The actual structure of the ﬂoor division can be seen on Figure 3.5 and the
detailed drawing can be seen with explanations and in scale in the prospectus
drawing 3.3.
Figure 3.5: House 1: Detail of the Bauma-deck used as ﬂoor separation within
the building.
Vertical division
The vertical bearing structures consists of facades and main partition walls.
All load bearing walls are solid masonry except at the top ﬂoor of the facades,
which are hollow walls with solid binders. The dimensions of each wall type can
be seen in Table 3.3. Similarly, the dimensions of the remaining non bearing
masonry walls of the building can be seen in the table. There are a few light
division walls of type double-walled walls aswell. The non-bearing masonry walls
supports themselves, whereas the lightweight partitions are carried by the ﬂoor
divisions.

Foundation
The foundation is a line foundation. There is insuﬃcient material in the archive

3.3.2
Structural calculations
The bearing capacity of the bearing elements of the existing building will now be
presented. There will not be made any calculations regarding the staircases, bal-
conies, nor the roof. Detailed calculations can be found in Appendix 3.2 to 3.9.
A short presentation of the calculation methods and the results are presented
in this thesis along with a discussion of their inﬂuence on a future renovation.
A section of the ﬂoor layout and measurements used for the calculation are
illustrated in Figure 3.6.

Horizontal division
As described in the registration section, the basement deck consists of 10 cm
concrete deck, and the remaining ﬂoors of 15 cm Bauma deck.
The static
conditions for the basement deck are not changed by adding a superstructure and
therefore it is not necessary to demonstrate the ability of the construction. The
same applies to the Bauma decks on the three ﬂoors of the building. However,
as the roof deck is also designed as a Bauma 15, it gives two possibilities for
a renovation solution.
Either the existing deck is used as ﬂoor in the new
construction, or a new deck can be added along with the superstructure. In
order to keep both possibilities open, the carrying capacity of the existing roof
deck must be proven to be suﬃcient for the greater load regarding residential
use.
Since Bauma decks are not used in construction today, it can be diﬃcult to
determine the bearing capacity by today’s calculation methods. This is due to
the fact that hollow-decks not used anymore and therefore calculation methods
have not been developed for current regulations.
==PAGE:46==
34
Case studies
In case of renovation it would be necessary to make tests of the existing decks
in order to document the carrying capacity. However, this is not an option in
this project. Instead, the manufacturer’s data sheets can be used to indicate
the carrying capacity.
The deck stretches across the building over the main partition wall. However, it
is not irrelevant how the connection over the wall is conducted. The connection
not only aﬀects the deck construction, but also the distribution of the load to
the combined walls. Therefore, before the calculations can be made it must be
determined whether the reinforcement is carried over the main partition wall
so that the entire deck works as one large beam or if it acts as two individual
beams. In Figure 3.8 the two possible connections are illustrated along with the
load distribution to the supports in case of equal subjects.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.8: The diﬀerent beam connections above the main partition wall.
Where the beam can be continued over the main partition wall
and function as one long beam (a) or function as two beams (b).

the connection above the main partition wall is examined. Based on the man-
ufacturer’s references, the reinforcement bents over the main partition wall as
illustrated in Figure 3.9. Which gives the beam eﬀect according to type (a),
thus the entire beam works as one long beam.

Wind
In order to calculate the bearing capacity of the walls and to make a stability
check of the building, the wind load must be determined, [mf.16]. The wind
can either aﬀect the facades or the gables. Wind load on the facades, ie. the
transversely direction of the building, is the most critical. Therefore, only this
situation will be analyzed and the calculations and design values can be seen in
Appendix 3.3. Depending on which calculation is carried out, the correlation
between wind pressure on the wind side and the lee side must be taken into
account. This is the reason for using two diﬀerent values for the ﬁnal wind load.
One for elements and one for the stability check where the correlation is taken
into account.

Eccentricity
Before determining the vertical bearing elements, an analysis of the diﬀerent
eccentricities that work on the diﬀerent walls must be made. The eccentricities
are determined on basis of [Sta15], where the theory and formulas used will be
summarized brieﬂy below.
The total eccentricity, et, consists of diﬀerent contributions from some of these
the eccentricity in the top of the wall can be determined. Similarly, the residing
and largest eccentricity occurs in the middle third of the wall and this is found
from the top eccentricity and the resisting eccentricity contributions.

center of gravity, e3, is set to 10 mm in all cases corresponding to increased
control. Likewise, e5 is set to 10 mm for all cases.
The decks are assembled in such a way, that they work as one individual beam

Vertical division, bearing
The load of the walls are determined at the top of the wall. Eg. at the ﬁrst
ﬂoor, the load from the ﬂoor divisions above and upwards are calculated. The
weight of the relevant ﬂoor is thus included in the consecutive ﬂoor. In other
words, it is only in the calculations of the foundation that the self-weight of the
==PAGE:54==

basement walls are included. The load and the bearing capacity are determined

archive material from the building archive of Hjørring municipality [Hjo17].
The total load can be seen in Appendix 3.5, where the critical load combination
is found.

The facade walls on the top ﬂoor is not a massive wall, but a cavity wall consist-

The bearing capacity is determined for a 1 meter massive wall, without taking
windows and doorways into account.
Stability made by cross-walls has nei-
ther been taken into account, which would give a signiﬁcantly higher carrying
capacity.
The fact that windows and doors are ignored in this analysis increases the
resulting bearing capacity. Vice versa this analysis disregards the existence of
cross walls, evidently decreasing the calculated capacity. Thus the calculations
assuming a massive straight piece for the most critical load explains a realistic
overview of the ratio between load and capacity.

Foundation
As described previously in Section 3.3.1 the foundation is a line foundation.
However, there is insuﬃcient material to make a full bearing calculation of the
foundation. Therefore, the loads on the foundation are determined in Appendix
3.7, but the bearing capacity cannot be checked. During an actual renovation,
drill samples would be made together with an investigation of the dimensions
and the state of the existing foundations. Taking this into account along with
the loads listed in Table 3.9, a check of the bearing capacity should be made
together with an analysis of the foundation.

Stability
Before a stability check can be performed, the wind load acting on the diﬀerent
stabilizing walls must be mapped. As a basis, the wind load aﬀects the entire
surface of the facades as a ﬂat load. To be able to check the stability the eﬀects
on each stabilizing wall must be determined. The procedure for this is listed
below:
• The wind load is assembled to line loads acting on each stabilizing wall
• The line load is converted to point loads acting at each deck. The deck
transfers the load to the stabilizing walls.
• The moment that occurs in the wall disk is determined as illustrated in
Figure 3.13. It is important to remember the impact of the overlaying
wall disks. Thus an additional link is added each time we move a ﬂoor
downwards.
==PAGE:57==
3.3 House 1, traditional building
45
When the moment is determined, the moment capacity must be determined
such that the stability can be checked. Determination of the moment capacity
is pointed out below:
• The dead load acting on each wall disk is determined
• Vertical bearing capacity is determined by Equation 3.6 without the area
link. The signiﬁcance of the area is introduced later in the calculations,
so it is not ignored.
• The minimum area is determined by a minimum lenght, as illustrated in
Figure 3.13.
• The eccentricity is determined based on the minimum length.
• The moment capacity is determined and checked by the acting moment.
The stability analysis is made for the wind exposed on the facades. The facades
are more exposed to wind than the gables because of the long facades and the
main partition walls. When the facades are exposed to wind all the transverse
walls will in principle contribute to obtain the wind load. The building consists
of 3/4 stones ﬁre- and ﬂat division walls and 1/2 stones light partition walls.
The calculations can be made either where all walls are taken into account or
only with some of the walls.
==PAGE:58==
46
Case studies
Figure 3.13: Absorption of the wind load in the stabilizing walls. Left, the
total wall plate is illustrate and one ﬂoor marked which red which
is show to the right. To illustrate the loads acting on each ﬂoor.
First a check is made to identify if the ﬁre- and ﬂat division walls obtains the
load equally. In this analysis the ﬂat division walls are found to be the critical
ones because they are the shortest. This check shows that there is not enough
capacity in the ﬂat division walls to distribute half the wind. Therefor a second
check is made, where the ﬁre walls obtain all the load.
This check gives a
maximum utilization of 78% for the most critical ﬁre wall. The stabilizing walls
in the second check are illustrated on Figure 3.14 together with the maximum
load width, which is used for the calculations as the inﬂuence of the gable walls
are ignored.

Overview
If the roof deck should be used for residential purposes, it would require tests to
determine if the carrying capacity is suﬃcient. Since the amount of reinforce-
ment varies throughout the deck, it would be diﬃcult to prove that the carrying
capacity is suﬃcient throughout the entire deck. It would therefor be easier to
establish a new deck in connection with the superstructure.
The stability study gives a maximum utilization of almost 79%. Although it is at
the high end, it is assumed that there is suﬃcient stability for a superstructure.
This is because the calculations is very conservative, as the contribution from
the gable walls are not taken into account, which would lower the utilization.
The analysis of the walls are the most comprehensive but also the most essential
component regarding whether a superstructure is realistic without extensive
==PAGE:60==
48
Case studies
renovation of the excising conditions.
The main partition wall is very congested in fact up to 145%. If a superstructure
is to be carried out, no additional load must be applied to the main partition
wall. It is therefore necessary either to provide very long decks that span all the
way across the building, or to set up an intermediate structure that transfers
the load from the superstructure away from the main partition wall and to the
facades. Likewise, the staircase walls are very overloaded and the gable walls are
slightly overloaded. Whereas the ﬁre and ﬂat division walls have a little residual
capacity with a maximum utilization of 84%. However, it is not suﬃcient to
carry a superstructure. Therefore, it depends on the facades, which are already
overloaded in the upper two ﬂoors due to a very high eccentricity from the wind
load. This eccentricity is determined by Equation 3.8. As seen, the eccentricity
depends on the size of the overlaying load N, the superstructure may therefor
work in favor of the existing structure.
e6 = M
N
(3.8)
The optimal relation with the superstructure will occur if the relation in Equa-
tion 3.8 makes zero so the eccentricity from the wind load is repealed. This
optimal situation is calculated in Appendix 3.9 and shown in Table 3.11. It can
be seen, that in this case the capacity increases signiﬁcantly for the two upper
ﬂoors, which are no longer overloaded. But even in this ﬁctitious situation which
illustrates the best possible situation, the maximum utilization is still 98%.
It must therefore be concluded that a superstructure for this building is only
possible if the existing structure is reinforced or a completely new static system
is established to carry the superstructure.

that even in this perfect situation, there is almost non residual

Main structure
The geometry of the building is a rectangular block with nine entrances. Bi-
cycles, prams, air-raid shelters, etc are placed in the basement. The remaining
ﬂoors are used for residential purposes. The transverse inner walls functions as
the bearing walls together with columns in the facade. The load bearing walls
and columns transfers the load to the foundation. The apartments have the
same design throughout the entire building, as seen on Figure 3.17.
(a) Floor level
==PAGE:64==

Case studies
(b) Basement level
Figure 3.17: House 2: Basement and ﬂoor level.
Roof
The building has a ﬂat roof top with a 6.5 cm polypropylene plate and covered
with three layers of built-up cladding. The drainage is constructed as internal
drains. The load from the roof is absorbed by all the bearing transverse inner
walls and the columns. Above each staircase there is a cupola skylight window.
Furthermore, the most signiﬁcant height dimensions can be seen on Figure 3.18.
Figure 3.18: House 2: Elevation drawing with heights used in the calculations.
Horizontal division
The horizontal divisions consists of insitu casted concrete slabs. The slabs are
18 cm reinforced concrete, except above the basement where it is only 10 cm
thick. The roof deck is constructed in the same way as the other ﬂoors, that is
a 18 cm thick reinforced concrete slab. The slabs function as a double tensed
plate laying on the inner walls and the columns.
==PAGE:65==
3.4 House 2, insitu cast concrete
53
Vertical division
The vertical bearing structures consists of almost all the inner walls. All the
bearing walls are 15 cm thick on each ﬂoor, and without any reinforcements.
The bearing columns have the same dimensions in the entire height, measuring
200 mm X 450 mm and 200 mm X 840 mm depending on whether they are
located by the balcony side, or at the street side.
The facade elements are non bearing and consists of carpentry elements with
exterior clad of eternit.
Likewise, the gables are non bearing, and they are
constructed with insulated sandwich elements. The few light division walls have
diﬀerent designs depending on whether they are located by kitchen or bathroom.
Foundation
The foundation type is a combination of line and point foundation. The line
foundation is found below the bearing inner walls and the facades. The point
foundation is used below the columns. The documented information concerning
the foundation are minimal.
Similarly the information regarding soil condi-
tions and drill samples are sparse. The bearing capacity of the foundation will
therefore not be subject to further investigation. However, the loads on the
foundation will be determined.
Figure 3.19: House 2: Foundation plan for the house.

Horizontal division
The slab structures in this building are reinforced concrete cast on site. The
slab over the basement is 10 cm thick and the remaining slabs are 18 cm thick.
The load on the slab is determined on bases of the presented load combinations,
the calculations can be seen in Appendix 3.11. It serves as a double-sided plate
with an area similar to one apartment. The plate is supported by inner walls
and columns in the facades, see Figure 3.22.
==PAGE:68==
56
Case studies
Figure 3.22: House 2: Static system for deck plates. The system will be mir-
rored for apartments on the left side of the staircases. The purple
rectangles marks the columns.
Due to the complexity of the plate relative to dimensions and support condi-
tions, a common look at the plate, with e.g. modeling of break pattern theory,
will not provide an exact result of the plate. This theory is used in the exist-
ing calculations found in the archive material. In these calculations there are
conduced some assumptions. For example the eﬀect of the columns are ignored.
However, all the assumptions are crafted with a high safe margin. But, in this
project the residual capacity of the existing construction is of high interest,
when implementing a superstructure. It is therefore important to determine
the bearing capacity as precise as possible. So it was chosen to make a FEM
model of the plate, using ABAQUS. This gives a precise calculation of the load
distribution of the supporting walls and columns, as well as the moment force
on the plate. Basic information about the ABAQUS model is listed below:
Mesh: In the ABAQUS model a triangular elements, STRI3, is used. There
are 1582 elements used for the entire plate. The element distribution can
be seen on Figure 3.23a.
Deﬂection and deformation: To see if the supports are deﬁned correctly, the
deformation of the plate have been studied. On Figure 3.23b the deﬂection
can be seen, the blue color marks almost zero deﬂection. On that ﬁgure it
looks like the deﬂection is zero, on the right side of the columns. Therefor,
==PAGE:69==
3.4 House 2, insitu cast concrete
57
Figure 3.23c has been made, here it can be seen that there is a small
deformation on the right side of the column, zero deﬂection at the column
and a large deﬂection on the left side of the column.

for

Element sectioning and deformation ﬁgures from

maintained at the supports and columns.
Before the result can be drawn from ABAQUS, it is important to check the
model to see if it is modeled properly. This is done by checking the reaction
in one of the columns, by a simple hand calculation. A upper and lower limit
of the reaction is determined, depending on how big an area of the plate the
column carries. The minimum area is marked on Figure 3.24 along with the
maximum area. The reaction is found by multiplying the area with the surface
load acting on the deck. If the model is correctly modeled, the equation will be
fulﬁlled:
Rmin < RABAQUS < Rmax
(3.9)
The results are illustrated below, showing that the conditions are met and the
model is thus approved, and can be used in the following calculations. Related
calculations can be found in Appendix 3.12.
49.8 kN < 52.2 kN < 56.7 kN OK!

Overview
Capacity check of the deck construction shows that the roof slabs have suﬃ-
cient bearing capacity to be used as deck in a superstructure. The walls have
incredibly high residual capacity with a maximum utilization of approximately
16%. This indicates that the thickness of the walls are not based on the load,
but rather in relation to the minimum requirements for ﬁre and sound protec-
tion. This theory is conﬁrmed by checking the surrounding ﬁve-story buildings
built together with this building. The wall dimensions in these buildings are
also 15 cm, although the load is 40% larger. This residual capacity provides
good opportunities for establishing a superstructure without any changes in the
existing construction.
The situation relating the columns are the same as for the walls; they have a
maximum utilization of approximately 14%. The dimensions of the columns
are equivalent to those in the ﬁve storey surrounding buildings. This means
that the columns have the capacity to carry by a superstructure. But since the
walls have such high residual capacity, it might not be necessary to extend the
columns up in the superstructure. However, this also depends on which facade
solutions are chosen for the superstructure and how it is ﬁxed.
The stability study also shows good results. The maximum utilization is found
==PAGE:79==
3.5 House 3, concrete elements
67
at the top ﬂoor at about 83% for check 1.
However, a superstructure will
work in favor of the construction, and its utilization will therefor be reduced
by establishing a superstructure. The superstructure, on the other hand, will
cause a higher moment due to the increased wind load, which aﬀects check 2
in the stability study. But, as the superstructure also causes greater dead load,
it is assumed that the ratio e = M/N does not change suﬃciently to make the
stability critical. Unlike the ﬁrst two checks, where an increase of the dead load
work in favor of the construction, this is not in favor for check 3. However, with
an utilization of only 7.7% at the existing conditions there is no risk related to
establishing a superstructure.
It can therefore be concluded that the building has suﬃcient bearing- and sta-
bilizing capacity in order to establish a superstructure without changes in the
existing structures conditions. This is only when looking at the capacity. The
requirements for ﬁre safety must also be meet, which will be investigated in
Section 3.6.
3.5
House 3, concrete elements
The building is from 1972 and placed in Helsingør, Zealand. It was built as
an individual building, but with nine equivalent buildings.
The ten buildings
were built in two laps: eight in the ﬁrst, and two buildings in the second. The
investigated building was constructed in the second step along with a four ﬂoored
building. First step consist of ﬁve buildings with four ﬂoors, and three buildings
with eight ﬂoors. The reason that the height of the buildings varies is due to the
inﬂuence of shadows and sunlight.

Main structure
The geometry of the building is a rectangular block with 4 entrances.
The
basement is used for bicycles, prams, air-raid shelters etc and all the remaining
ﬂoors are used for residents. The bearing walls consists of the transverse inner
walls and they lead the load to the foundation. The design of the apartments
are the same throughout the entire building as seen on Figure 3.31.
(a) Floor level
(b) Basement level
Figure 3.31: House 3: Basement and ﬂoor layout.
Roof
There is a ﬂat roof top on the building, made out of asphalt, polyhor plate
7.5cm, 1.5 cm wood concrete and 3 layers of rooﬁng felt on the top. All the
bearing transverse inner walls are together of absorbing the load from the roof.
Each staircase have a skylight window as seen on Figure 3.32 together with the
most signiﬁcant dimensions.

3.5 House 3, concrete elements
71
Figure 3.32: House 3: Elevation drawing of House 3, with essential height
measurements used throughout the calculations.
Horizontal division
The horizontal division above the basement is a 8 cm thick concrete plate. The
rest of the horizontal divisions including the roof division is 18 cm perforated
plates reinforced per each 15 cm. The plates function as a single tensed plate
laying on the transverse inner walls.
Vertical division, bearing
All the vertical divisions are constructed as prefabricated elements. The vertical
bearing inner walls consist of 15 cm thick concrete walls without any reinforce-
ment. Except for the gable walls which are 20 cm thick. The facades are not a
part of the bearing structure and they are designed as sandwich elements. The
few light divisions consists of 6.4 cm thick concrete walls.
Foundation
Pile foundation are used as foundation type in this building. In addition to the
other buildings the carrying capacity of the foundation will not be conducted
due to the limitation of information. However, the loads that are transferred to
the foundation will be determined.

In order to make a capacity calculation of the deck plates, to assess if the roof
deck can be used in conjunction with a superstructure, some information about
the structure are necessary.
The design of the tire is not found in the archive material, the same applies
to the reinforcement type and strength. This is due to the fact, that all the
dimensions have been made according to the manufacturer’s reference, which
was standard practice at the time of construction. However, it has not been
possible to ﬁnd information from the manufacturer or load capacity tables, as
the company has changed its name and ownership repeatedly and does not exist
today.
It could be chosen to make some assumptions for the missing data and then
calculate a likely bearing capacity. However, this part is not essential for this
project and therefore omitted.

taken into account, this is not the case for the load used to check the elements
bearing capacity.

Overview
As the result for House 3 is very similar to the result of House 2, they are only
shortly discussed. The deck capacity is not checked because of lack of informa-

the capacity must be checked during renovation. The residual capacity of the
walls are very big, as the dimension is determined based on ﬁre requirements
and not load aﬀection. This gives favourable conditions for a superstructure.
The maximum utilization is a bit higher than that of House 2, with a maximum
utilization in check 1 at 105%. However, a superstructure works in favor of
check 1 and the result is therefor not critical.
Overall, the results points to a conclusion, that there are suﬃcient bearing and
stabilizing capacity in order to establish a superstructure without changes in
the existing conditions. If so the ﬁre safety is also suﬃcient for House 3.
3.6
Fire
In Chapter 2 the diﬀerent laws and regulations where introduced and the re-
quirements for the three cases listed. Therefor, the three houses are all checked
to see if they fulﬁll the ﬁre safety regulations. As it was concluded in Chapter
2 the development and tightening of the ﬁre safety regulations throughout the
time period 1950-1980 is not signiﬁcant, although some changes where incorpo-
rated. The law and regulations have been more accurate and the level of details
to prevent ﬁre has been increased. This indicates that all the houses can be
checked equally even though House 1 is build 16 years earlier.
From the Building Regulations in 1961 and forward there is a demand for rescue
openings on each ﬂoor in each apartment. These openings must be placed so
that there is free access for the ﬁre service and they must have a certain size.
==PAGE:91==
3.6 Fire
79
Figure 3.38 shows the rescue openings for the three cases with dimensions to
illustrate that the sizes are fulﬁlled. It should be mentioned that the rescue
windows in all cases are tilt windows, therefor the eﬃcient opening height is
only half of the total height. The openings can all be reached by the ﬁre service
since they are facing the road side of the buildings.
(a) House 1
(b) House 2
(c) House 3
Figure 3.38: Rescue opening marked on one staircase section for each house.
Dimensions for the openings are the same for each apartment in
each staircase section. All the rescue openings fulﬁlls the require-
ments of the construction time.
Likewise, the Building Regulations in 1961 demands that the staircase is at
minimum 1 meter wide with a high of 2 meters and has a smoke hatch (1m2).
Furthermore, there most be free access to open air through a door spanning at
least 0.9x2.0 meters which opens in the escape direction without any lock. All
these requirements are fulﬁlled for the three houses.
The Building Regulations in 1966 required that the buildings must not be placed
closer than 2.5 meters from neighboring boundaries. Another demand is that
the apartment doors must be BD-entredoor-30 and BD-door-60 for the base-
ment. The mentioned regulation is more detailed than the previous ones. The
requirements for the doors are not more strict just more accurate. It is like-

3.7
Discussion
In the previous sections, the three cases have been reviewed, both concerning
bearing capacity and ﬁre conditions. Together, the three buildings form the
basis of building practice for the period 1950-1980 as described in Chapter 2.
The residual capacity in House 1 is limited, and thus the possibilities for expan-
sion are also limited. The lack of residual bearing capacity is mainly due to the
==PAGE:95==
3.7 Discussion
83
overloaded main partition wall and the hollow brick wall on the top ﬂoor of the
facades. However, there is also not enough residual capacity in the transverse
walls to contribute to the carrying capacity of any extension. It is therefore
necessary to reinforce the existing structures if a new superstructure becomes a
reality.
This limitation will be a typical problem for all properties built based on the
same principles as for House 1, as well as buildings with a small variation in
construction methods related to House 1. Such a property is actually built in
extension of House 1 a few years later, refered to as House 4.
The diﬀerence between the two house types are the roof construction.
The
diﬀerent constructions for the two houses are listed in Table 3.22. This diﬀerence
is due to further optimizing of the building. While the roof deck in House 1 is
not used.
The diﬀerence between the two buildings does not have a great inﬂuence in
relation to a future renovation. This is due to the fact that the renovation is
governed by the minimum residual bearing capacity in the walls which are the
same for both buildings. The main diﬀerence is that House 4 does not have the
free roof deck as House 1.
Table 3.22: House 1: House 1 could be built in a slightly diﬀerent way (House
4). The table shows that the diﬀerences leis in the roof construc-
tion and the rest of the building is identical for both house types.

Facade
