Masonry
Masonry
Unlike masonry construction represented by House 1, the result is completely
diﬀerent for concrete constructions. House 2 and 3, which represent cast on site
and element buildings in concrete respectively, both have a very good foundation
for a superstructure. This is because both the bearing capacity and the stability
have suﬃcient residual carrying capacity.
All three houses fulﬁll the existing ﬁre requirements. However, it is not certain
that the ﬁre requirements are still met after the renovation for the existing
building part, this will be investigated and explained later.
==PAGE:96==
84
Case studies
Based on registration and calculations for the three buildings investigated, it
can therefore be concluded that concrete buildings are a much better starting
point structurally and are more suitable for a superstructures than masonry
constructions during the period of concern.
==PAGE:97==
Chapter 4
Concept development
This section will present the considerations, which have provided the basis for
the design choice of the superstructures in the three cases. With the focus on the
materials, building styles, and structural design that will connect the existing
building with the expansion. Likewise, a presentation of the building require-
ments, which must be fulﬁll, are given with particular focus on the ﬁre safety.
4.1
Building style and material options
Before a superstructure design can be created, there are many considerations
that needs to be reviewed. Some of these considerations concern the choice of
materials. Selection of materials can be based on all from environmental- to
technical aspects. Furthermore, one has to explore diﬀerent building styles to
ﬁnd the one, which is best ﬁtted in the concerning case. Diﬀerent aspects for
choice of materials and building style are listed below:
Technical
• Weight
• Fire Resistance
==PAGE:98==
86
Concept development
• Merging with existing part
Practical
• Need for rehousing
• Construction time
• Transportation
• Maintenance
• Availability
Economical
• Construction cost
• Material cost
• Maintenance cost
Environmental
• CO2 ﬁngerprint
• Durability
• Sustainability
• Energy consumption
When constructing a new building, the choice of material can be made more
freely. However, in renovation it is necessary, ﬁrst and foremost, to take the
existing construction into account.
Therefore, the most important aspect is
based on the limited residual capacity, which aﬀects the allowable weight of the
superstructure. However, the weight cannot be too small as it can cause stability
problems due to the increasing wind load. In addition, it is also important to
look at the fact, that there are existing residents in the building. Considerations
about this are not only important regarding out of respect towards the residents,
but also in relation to ﬁnances. Because, it can be an expensive aﬀair, if it is
necessary to have a large and long rehousing of the residents. In short, when
renovating, the choice of materials and building style will therefor mainly be
based on:
• Low weight
• Fire resistance
• Need for rehousing
• Quick construction time on site
==PAGE:99==
4.1 Building style and material options
87
Since the three buildings represent three diﬀerent building methods for the time
period 1950-1980, no common solution can be developed to use for all three
houses. Since they diﬀer on several points, for example the static system. As
Chapter 3 has shown, there is no signiﬁcant residual bearing capacity in House
1. On the other hand, there is a very high residual capacity within House 2 and
3. The demand for low weight of the superstructure is therefor mostly important
for House 1, whereas the demand for fast construction time and low need for
rehousing are essential for all three houses.
One could start by listing various types of building materials together with their
advantages / disadvantages. But the wish for short construction time and low
rehousing are more associated with the building style than the type of material.
Typical building styles can, in rough terms, be divided into three steps;
• Elements
• Surface elements
• Module/box elements
It goes without saying, that when a larger part of the construction phase takes
place on the factory, the smaller time is needed on the site. Thus, a module/box
construction will cause a signiﬁcant lower construction time, than traditional el-
ement construction. In Denmark there are several diﬀerent companies perform-
ing module/box systems, but essential for all of them are that they use wood
for the bearing system. Wood, as a material, ﬁts well with the desire for a low
weight superstructure, on the other hand, wood is not the most optimal choice
regarding ﬁre resistance. Module/box systems are therefor the most suitable
for 3 out of 4 of the desired requirements. It is especially well suited for achiev-
ing a short rehousing and construction time. Whilst, the modules are lifted in
place with a crane and thus removing the need for a scaﬀold. What is more,
as a large part of the workmanship is performed at the factory, the noise on
the construction site is minimizes. Module/box systems in lumber are therefore
rated to be the most optimal building style, for constructing superstructures on
existing residential buildings. The ﬁre resistance will be achieved with covering.
The following section will shortly present the basic of module/box systems, the
technical information will be given in Chapter 5.
==PAGE:100==
88
Concept development
4.2
Module systems
There are several diﬀerent manufacturers of module systems in Denmark, one of
them is Scandi Byg A/S. Scandi Byg A/S has more than 30 years of experience
with module solutions. They have been assisting throughout this project by
answering questions and giving a tour in one of their production halls.
Module systems, as building type, have many advantages in addition to those
presented in the previous section.
Module construction conserves energy re-
sources throughout the entire construction period, due to the eﬃcient production-
line. However, the use of module building systems provides a greater energy
consumption on transport and for the cranes, than traditional construction.
However this consumption is lower than the part which is saved under the pro-
duction phase. The production phase is highly optimized, as materials, tools,
and construction workers are on the same location and thus do not need to be
moved around. Instead the modules are moved through the production hall as a
collection tie. During a tour in one of Scandi Byg’s production halls, the various
production steps were clear. As soon as one step is ﬁnished the modules are
moved to the next, where other workers takes over. In order to preserve business
concepts, it is not allowed to provide pictures from within the production hall.
However, a vague picture of the production-line was granted, and is shown in
Figure 4.1. The main concept of an in-house module production line can be
inspected at Figure 4.2.
Figure 4.1: Picture from Scandi Byg A/S production hall in Løgstør Denmark.
==PAGE:101==
4.2 Module systems
89
Figure 4.2: The steps of a factory based module production can be roughly
divided into 9 steps. Where each step has dedicated workers, in-
creasing eﬃciency.
The production-line illustrates the eﬃciency by the construction process and the
faster construction time. What is more, as the factory can operate regardless
of natural light, the steps can be executed in teams throughout the entire day,
vastly increasing the throughput. In addition, it is easier to ensure the denseness
of the construction, when it is carried out under controlled conditions in the
factory halls, mainly due to:
==PAGE:102==
90
Concept development
• The weather does not aﬀect the production
• The ergonomic conditions, for the workers, are optimal
• The right tools are always available
• The production details are well known
The use of timber as building material is not only an advantage in connection
with the load impact. Unlike all other building materials, wood binds CO2.
That means wood constructions stores a large amount of CO2 and wooden-
constructions are thereby more or less CO2 neutral during the construction
lifetime. The energy consumption of wood production and processing is very
low. Each m3 of lumber, which replaces another material is saving 1.1 ton of
CO2. Likewise, each m3 wood binds about 0.9 ton CO2. A wood based module
consumes approximately 0.33 m3 of lumber per 1 m3 ﬁnished construction. This
yields a saving of 0.66 ton CO2 per m3 construction, [Mod].
Module building systems consists roughly of large squared closed blocks. The
modules can have a maximum size of 5x14 meters, due to transportation as-
pects. But, if the width exceeds 4.2 meters, transportation with a support car
is needed, which increases costs. As shown in the production-line Figure 4.2, all
electricity, kitchen, bathrooms, windows, doors, ect. are installed in the ﬁnished
module. The work distribution is 3/1 between factory and the construction site,
ie. approx. 75% is carried out at the factory.
This might give the impression, that module systems is a mass production,
that have no ﬂexibility, but this is not the case. Because, even though large
parts of the construction process are recycled for each project, it is mainly the
engineering and technical solutions which are being recycled. The dimensions
and design of the modules are made for each project. Hence, there is ample
opportunity for the architects and developers to create an individual project.
This will be seen in the choice of superstructures for the three cases, as they
illustrates diﬀerent design options.
Scandi Byg complies with the Building Regulations, and they are energy-optimized
for low-energy class 2020. The costs for low-energy buildings are marginally
higher, than constructions that only meets the Building Regulations minimum
requirements. But a later renewal of the energy will be more costly, and a patch
solution are rarely as optimal, as if it was done from the start. Therefor Scandi
Byg’s modules are made to reach the 2020 energy requirements.
In order to reach full beneﬁt of module building, it is important to think about
module diversity. That is, if n modules are needed in a project and all n mod-
ules diverge in design the beneﬁt from the production line would be minimal.
==PAGE:103==
4.3 Fire safety
91
Opposing to make n identical modules. This is because the modules are dimen-
sioned from the bottom in each project, the total time consumed per module
thus consists of dimensioning, drawing, and production.
The more identical
modules that are being produced in a project, the shorter time are being used
for dimensioning, creating drawing material, and by the workers who will obtain
familiarity with the product. Hence, the total time consumption will mainly be
the production time. On Figure 4.3 it can be seen, that the time per module is
optimal at around 10 identical modules and on wards.
Figure 4.3: The time and cost per module is optimal when at least 10 identical
modules is produced at the same time.
4.3
Fire safety
Fire protection is an important issue when it comes to renovation.
This is
because when an existing building is extended, it is not enough that the new
building part satisﬁes modern ﬁre requirements, the existing construction must
meet the requirements as well. Since, it has no eﬀect that the upper ﬂoors are
secured if the bottom ﬂoors are not. In other words, the new superstructure
cannot meet the requirements unless the existing part also does. Therefore, it
is important to keep track of the existing ﬁre requirements at the time of the
settlement and what changes that are made in relation to today’s requirements.
The ﬁre requirements from the settlement date are already described in Section
2.2, and in this section they will be compared to the requirements of the Danish
Building Regulations 2015 [BR115].

is visible if the regulation was the same, partly present, or none

Table 4.1 gives a quick overview of the main diﬀerences between today’s building
regulations and the building regulations present at the time of origin. It is easily
seen, that most of the requirements are the same. The few places where they are
diﬀerent are mainly due to elevator requirements. In Table 4.1, two paragraphs
are marked as partly satisﬁed. This is because, in 5.5.2 pcs 1 the requirement for
elevator stands are missing. In 5.6.1 pcs 1-3, there is an additional requirement
for riser pipes, which is new.
But even though the requirements of the Building Regulations are mainly the
same, it does not mean that the existing building complies with today’s ﬁre
requirements. This is because the diﬀerence is not on the wording about the
presence of adequate ﬁre protection, but in the perception of what adequate
ﬁre protection is.
The industry can be documented in two ways, either as
prescriptive based ﬁre safety, which is based on "Eksempelsamlingen" [oB06] or
as performance based ﬁre safety; which is based on calculations according to
"Information om brandteknisk dimensionering" [oB04].
At the time of construction only documentation, based on prescriptive based ﬁre
safety, was possible. There were not yet evolved enough documentation about
ﬁre development, and therefor there were not yet any calculation models for
performance based ﬁre safety. This indicates, that the measurements required
to fulﬁll ﬁre safety has changed. Due to the fact, what it is possible now to
deviate from the normal standards, if one can document that the ﬁre safety is
still fulﬁlled. Also the perception of prescriptive based ﬁre safety are changed
through time.
Some conditions have not change since 1950-80, for example: The requirement
for 2 independent escape routes, the maximum ﬁre cell size of 150 m2, and the
maximum ﬁre section size of 600 m2, whilst the latter can now be increased by
sprinkler system, it is in its base form unchanged. On the other hand, the ﬁre
designations have changed. The term BD no longer exist, and the term BS has
been changed into a new designation system. The new system is more advanced
and contains a lot of new designations. The new terms and changes relative to
origin are described in DBI [DBI08]. Table 4.2 shows the comparison between
new and origin designations.
Fire protection of the existing building relative to modern requirements, will be
documented with the ﬁre safety of the new building in the Chapter 6. However,
as described above, one of the major changes are found in the requirements,
which follows when the building goes from three to a six leveled building. This
doubles the requirement of 60 minutes ﬁre resistance to 120 minutes. It also
requires, that it is necessary to establish an elevator in the building. Therefore,
a further description of elevators will be presented in the next section.
==PAGE:106==
94
Concept development

4.3.1
Elevators
Elevators in both existing and new buildings have many advantages. In existing
buildings where several ﬂoors are added, elevators will ﬁrst and foremost be
established due to comfort requirements.
The Building Regulations require
elevators in all storey buildings greater than three ﬂoors. But lifts have many
other advantages. They increase the value of the building, improve accessibility,
and increase the attractiveness of both tenants and buyers. Furthermore, they
provide a safer process in the building over stairs, and opens the possibility of
elderly residents to stay longer.
A lift can either be shut down under ﬁre or dimensioned for use in case of ﬁre.
Being able to use the elevator in case of ﬁre has many diﬀerent advantages for
the ﬁre protection depending on the layout and eﬀect. It can be used as a service
elevator and/or for evacuation of the residents in case of ﬁre.
In residential buildings, like the ones which are investigated in this report, the
number of people within the buildings are limited relative to eg. an oﬃce build-
ing. It is in these cases advantageous to use the elevator only as a service lift,
as it avoids conﬂict between the rescue service and the residents. This allows
residents to evacuate down the stairs and the rescue workers can be transported
up through the elevator. This prevents crossings in the elevators and on the
==PAGE:107==
4.4 Building Regulations 2015
95
stairs so that the rescue service can act undisturbed by the residents. As an ad-
dition to the above mentioned, the elevator can be used to transport people who
are not able to get out of the building by themselves. There may be situations
where it is necessary to use the elevator for evacuation, even though it is not a
part of the ﬁre strategy. Such a case may be that the ﬁre blocks the staircase of
the upper ﬂoor, and the elevator can hereby transport the occupants past the
ﬁre to safety. Since there is no 100% guarantee of where a ﬁre occurs and how
it is spread, you can come in situations where it is necessary to deviate from the
desired ﬁre strategy.
In each case, studied in the previous chapter, elevators will be installed inside or
outside the building, which are dimensioned according to the number of users.
It is chosen to locate the elevators in three diﬀerent ways for the three cases, to
give an overview of the beneﬁts of the diﬀerent solutions. In all cases elevators
are placed with dimensions, so they can be used as both service elevator and for
evacuation, if the related conditions allows it.
The size of the elevator shafts are dimensioned so that the four largest producers
of elevators in Denmark can be used. The four investigated producers are KONE
[A/S17], OTIS [Com17], Schindler [Sch17] and Thyssenkrupp [Thy17]. From
each company, the selection of lifts and data sheets has been collected i Appendix
4.1 to 4.4. All the four companies have each one model that they recommend
for existing construction. The models are available in diﬀerent sizes as needed.
4.4
Building Regulations 2015
When an extension is made, the output will be a combination of existing and
new constructions. Therefore, there are diﬀerent laws and regulations that apply
to the diﬀerent parts of the building. If no signiﬁcant renovation or modiﬁcation
of use is made on the existing building, this part must not comply with today’s
requirements, but instead from the demands of the building origin. However,
when doing any intervention, it is a good idea to upgrade the existing parts to
new standards, as far as possible. In addition, all new units passing through the
existing building as the elevator, must comply with applicable requirements.
Several parts of the building regulation deals with the building as one unit. This
is reﬂected in the ﬁre protection as an example. It does not matter that the three
top ﬂoors meets the ﬁre regulations, if the three bottom ﬂoors does not. Fur-
thermore, a superstructure will aﬀect the excising bearing parts of the building,
and they must therefor also be checked for present requirements. Therefore, it is
important to connect new and old together, so that the new part exceeds all ex-
==PAGE:108==
96
Concept development
isting laws and the existing part also complies with the requirements to a certain
extent, such that the building in one can meet the demands. The relevant ﬁre
requirements for the buildings investigated, in this report, are described in the
previous section, so they will not be presented here. Other relevant requirements
will be brieﬂy described, based on the Danish Building Regulations [BR115]. Al-
though the entire building regulations are not described, the remaining parts
must also be observed. As this thesis does not concern the energy perspectives
of the building, these regulations will not be described.
BR15: 3. Building layout
• 3.2.1 pcs 2+4: The access to the building units and the elevator must
be in level. Doors must have a minimum width of 0.77 meter.
• 3.2.2 pcs 1+3+4: The common access routes shall be minimum 1.3
meter width. Staircases must have a minimum width of 1 meter and
a free height of 2.1 meter, with a maximum increase of 18 cm and a
ground of 25 cm.
• 3.2.3 pcs 1: Stairways must be provided with a railing or other pro-
tection.
• 3.3.1 pcs 2+5: A residential must contain a living space, a kitchen
and a bath/WC-room. The free room height must be minimum 2.5
meters.
BR15: 4. Structures
• 4.1 pcs 2: Building structures must be dimensioned for static and
dynamic impacts.
• 4.2 pcs 1-4: Dimensioning must be based on Eurocodes with associ-
ated Danish annexes.
BR15: 8. Installations
• 8.8 pcs 2: There must be natural or mechanical ventilation of the
elevator shaft. The machine room must be ventilated as well.
• The elevator must as a minimum meet the requirements for energy
class B.
How the requirements from the Danish Building Regulations are fulﬁlled will
be described in the following chapters.
In which, the documentation of the
solutions will be carried out and the ﬁnal solutions will be presented. In case of
a rebuilding, the provisions may be adjusted if an application for dispensation
are made. Due to the fact, that a fulﬁllment of the requirements will implicate
a major intervention in the existing structure, all the requirements will as far as
possible, be fulﬁlled without any dispensations, and if dispensation is necessary
an explanation will be given in why.
==PAGE:109==
Chapter 5
Static veriﬁcation
Throughout this chapter the static veriﬁcation of the superstructures will be pre-
sented.
Additionally, this chapter will present some reinforcement solutions,
regarding lack of residual bearing capacity in House 1, such that a superstruc-
ture can be carried out. For House 2 and 3 the residual bearing capacity is not
an issue. In both cases one solution are determined, wherein the modules of
the superstructures are designed and calculated. The modules structure are the
same in both cases, therefore the calculations for House 2 and 3 will be presented
together.
5.1
House 1
As concluded in the previous chapter, this type of building does not have suﬃ-
cient residual bearing capacity, for a superstructure, without a major interven-
tion. For this reason, a resolute resolution will not be elaborated or prepared in
this case. Instead, a solution will be described, on how the bearing problems can
be solved by a more circumstanced renovation, if this is desired.
==PAGE:110==
98
Static veriﬁcation
Reinforcement of fully loaded facade wall
The problem, regarding the lack of residual bearing capacity in the facades, is
solely due to the cavity wall on the top ﬂoor. This wall has a signiﬁcantly lower
carrying capacity, than the remaining ﬂoors.
Furthermore, it creates a high
eccentricity on the underlying ﬂoor.
Therefore, in order to place a superstructure directly on the existing structure,
it is necessary to reinforce the upper part of the facades. This can be done by
eg. making internal cross-reinforcements, of the hollow wall on the 2ND ﬂoor
and the massive wall on the 1ST ﬂoor. The reinforcement can be established in
connection with an internal re-insulation. Since it is a rather major intervention,
it is important that the gain is maximized. By incorporation the reinforcement
in a post-insulation of the facades, it is not only the carrying capacity account
that is increased, but also on the energy account. Such reinforcement can be
established by using steel Z-proﬁles as outlined in Figure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: Cross-reinforcement sketch of a hollow brick wall using Z-proﬁles
built together with an inner re-insulation.
Another solution for making a superstructure possible is not to use the exist-
ing static system. But, to establish a new static system outside the existing
structure. The support system could consist of columns, that could either be
established throughout the entire height or supported at each ﬂoor separation,
==PAGE:111==
5.1 House 1
99
depending on the design. Similarly, to the previous solution this can be hidden,
in an external post-insulation and thereby provide an energy improvement as
well. This solution will however cause the existing windows to be moved out,
to ﬁt the new facades.
Transfer system
Although the facades are reinforced and have suﬃcient load bearing capacity for
a superstructure, the whole problem is not resolved. This is proportional to the
already overloaded main partition wall. It is important that this is not loaded
further, i.e. all new weights must be led to the facades. As the building is more
than 11 meters wide, it requires large horizontal division structures to be able to
span all the way. Another solution is to establish an inter-construction between
the existing construction and new construction that moves all the load from the
new construction to the facades. Thereby, it is not necessary for the horizontal
divisions to span the entire width of the building.
This can be solved by using a steel grid or beam system, that spans between the
facades. The grid/ beam system takes up the load from the superstructure and
transports it further to the facades. This solution requires a fairly large grid/
beam system, but similarly the dimension on the remaining horizontal divisions
can be signiﬁcantly reduced.
The solution which is most advantageous, most be determined in relation to the
materials, construction time, price, etc. such that the most optimal solution is
found. This matter is not subject to discussion in this thesis
Superstructure
The fact that the existing static system does not have suﬃcient residual bearing
capacity, to support an superstructure, is not in itself a negative factor.
It
provides greater freedom in relation to the visual design and choice of materials.
This is because that there is no existing inner walls that needs to be continued
to accommodate the new load. As described above, it can be chosen to reinforce
the existing facades, this introduces a limitation of the possibilities as it will not
be possible to supersede the existing plane. But if it is chosen to produce a
completely new static system, there are no restrictions to establish a diﬀerent
ﬂoor plan than the existing one. However, account must be taken towards, for
for example shadows in relation to the existing construction etc. Thereby, the
bearing obstacles contributes a greater ﬂexibility in relation to the architectural
==PAGE:112==
100
Static veriﬁcation
design of the superstructure. This opens better opportunities to make the new
apartments optimal, for all types of residents, handicapped, old people, or other
groups with reduced mobility. As only the facades are being continued and the
decor is therefore not locked by existing inner walls.
The expansion can be
made by everything from individual elements to surface elements or even large
modular/box systems. An example of how such an extension could be designed
will be presented later in the report.
5.2
House 2 & House 3
The superstructures for House 2 and 3 are based on Scandi Byg A/S model
system. However, they cannot give any structural engineering material on the
modules or detail drawings, as it does not comply to company policies. However,
they have some basic guidelines, that they follow when designing models:
• A module can span a maximum size of 4.2 m x 14 m (exceptional cases
5x14m).
• A module must be a square.
• The bearing span is on the short side.
• It should use structural timber C24, if necessary Kerto-beams for extra
capacity.
• A module weights about 12-17 ton.
• A module must have a timber frame with bars per 400 mm or 600 mm.
• Fire safety is obtained by use of ﬁre gypsum.
• Staircases are ordered externally, same goes for elevators and galleries.
• Module divisions are optimally placed at apartment divisions.
• Any facade types can be used.
• Keep the design simple.
Based on the guidelines, a design for each critical building part in the modules
are determined. As for the existing building parts, the parts of the superstruc-
tures are controlled by the four load combinations. Furthermore, the modules
are checked for ﬁre in the next chapter. The module parts are designed for the
==PAGE:113==
5.2 House 2 & House 3
101
two houses, the essential results are presented. The elements in the modules are
proportioned along with a transfer systems between the existing construction
and the new construction. The connections between the new and old build-
ing parts will be described, however the connections within the superstructure
will not, since if the superstructure is actually executed, these connections will
be conducted according to Scandi Byg’s procedures. Data sheets for the used
materials can be found in Appendix 5.1 to 5.4.
5.2.1
Structural system
First of all, the ﬂoors in the superstructure must be divided into modules that
meet the requirements listed above. A solution has been chosen for House 2 with
three identical ﬂoors and for House 3 with varying ﬂoor plans. The background
for these choices and the layout of the superstructure will be presented together
with the design and ﬂoor plans in Chapter 7. This chapter will only cover the
control of the bearing capacity.
House 2 consists of nine entrances, but only three of these will be lead up into
the superstructure. The property can therefore be divided into three identical
sections. The section is illustrated in Figure 5.2, where the module layout is
marked with pink on top of the existing ﬂoor plan. It is seen, that the new
ﬂoor plans get a smaller area as a gallery is established. While this building is
over 12 m wide and therefore could not comply with the desired length of the
modules, it is chosen to involve some of the staircases for apartments instead,
which also provides an optimal solution for large-format modules. The division
consists of three diﬀerent module sizes marked in Figure 5.2, their dimensions
are shown in Table 5.1. The reason for module 1a and 1b is due to diﬀerent
decor, however the modules have the same size.

5.2.4
Transfer system
To transfer the load form the superstructure to the existing bearing walls, a
transfer system needs to be constructed.
In both cases it is therefor chosen
to use steel beams, which are placed between the existing roof and the new
superstructure. The existing roofs are preserved and will function as ﬁre-section
between the 3TH and 4Th ﬂoor.
The connection between the modules and the steel beams are investigated, along
with the connection between the existing roof and the steel beams. Both con-
nections can be seen in scale with explanations in the Prospectus, drawing 5.8
and 5.10 for House 2 and 5.9 and 5.11 for House 3.
==PAGE:119==

The modules have to be insured against overturning and sliding. Therefore a
steel plate connected to the steel beams and the modules are applied, to establish
vertical anchorage. Horizontally the superstructure is secured by a threaded rod,
attached to the existing roof construction spanning all the way up to the new
rooftop. The steel beams will be connected to the existing building by use of
glue anchors.

5.2.5
Existing building parts
The load on the bearing inner walls for both houses are changed when adding a
superstructure, due to the increased load. Therefore the walls most be checked
for the new load. The calculations can be seen in Appendix 5.29 and 5.30. The
most critical wall for House 2 is wall 3 and in House 3 wall 1. The result are
illustrated in Table 5.3. The bearing capacity before and after the superstructure
diﬀers slightly, because of the change in eccentricity. For House 2 the three
upper ﬂoors weights approximately 44% of the total weight of the lower ﬂoors.
In House 3 the ratio is 35%. The diﬀerence occurs, since House 3 consists of
varying ﬂoor plans with a smaller area.

5.3
Elevators
As described in the previous section, a suitable elevator from each of the four
largest manufacturers in Denmark have been collected. Although the elevator
==PAGE:123==
5.3 Elevators
111
solution is diﬀerent for the three properties, the requirements for them are the
same. The buildings belongs to the category low and mid rise buildings, which
means that the speed must not exceed 1.6 m/s. Furthermore, if an elevator is
used as a ﬁre ﬁghter lift it must have a minimum size of 1.2m x 1.4 m and must
take a minimum load of 630 kg. However, if used for evacuation the size should
be at least 1.1 m x 2.1 m and a sustain a load of 1000 kg.
In House 1, the lifts are built outside the property connected to a gallery. There
are 2 lifts for the entire property. These elevators can be used as both ﬁre ﬁghter
lifts and evacuation lifts.
For House 2 an elevator is established per three entrances. That is, three eleva-
tors are set up for the building. The lifts are established in an independent ﬁre
section inside the property and they can therefore be used both as ﬁre ﬁghter
lifts and evacuation lifts.
For House 3, an elevator is established in each of the existing four entrances. The
lifts are established in connection to the stairs, which means that the ﬁre section
of the staircase and the ﬁre section of the elevator is connected. Therefore, the
elevators in House 3 must not be used for rescue service or evacuation in case
of ﬁre.
For convenience, the same size of elevator are used for all three houses, although
House 3 could use a smaller elevator as it should not be used in case of ﬁre. Data
for the selected elevators are listed in Table 5.4.

All the elevator models is shipped in a self-supporting shaft, but this shaft is not
ﬁreproof. A ﬁreproof shaft must therefore be established around the lifts to en-
sure, that the requirements for the ﬁre section is fulﬁlled. At the same time, the
==PAGE:124==
112
Static veriﬁcation
new lifts in House 2 and 3 breaks the existing load-bearing walls. The elevator
shaft therefore overtakes the load from the existing ﬂoor separations. Due to the
design of the transfer system, the elevator shafts do not receive any load from
the superstructures. The load impact on the elevator shafts are therefore less
than for the existing walls. Which means that a shaft of 150 mm concrete walls
will have the suﬃcient carrying capacity and no check is necessary. To meet the
requirements of a REI120 ﬁre section, the walls must be at least 150 mm thick
with a minimum of 25 mm cover layer according to [Sta13a]. The shafts are
thereby made of 15 mm thick concrete walls with two layers of reinforcement
with a 25 mm cover layer. In order to obtain the load from the existing ﬂoor
separation, consoles to carrying the slabs are established, as illustrated in Figure
5.7, the connection can be seen in scale and with explanations on Prospectus
5.14.
Figure 5.7: House 2 & 3: Connection between existing deck construction and
the new elevator shaft. Used because the elevator takes up some
of the existing bearing walls.
==PAGE:125==
Chapter 6
Fire strategy
This section will include an overall ﬁre safety strategy, and a structural ﬁre
safety determination for each of the cases. The section will be split into two
main parts.
The ﬁrst part is concerning the pre-ﬂashover phase, which has the overall pur-
pose of documenting the safety of people doing a potential evacuation. This is
satisﬁed by investigating the evacuation time in conjunction with the critical
time for smoke inhalation and lack of visibility.
The second part of this chapter is intended for the post-ﬂashover phase, which
has the overall purpose of documenting the structural ﬁre safety. This is satisﬁed
by investigating the bearing capacity and the accidental load, at the time of the
resistance class.
6.1
Fire analysis of House 1
Since House 1 did not provide enough residual capacity to sustain a superstruc-
ture, no speciﬁc solution was designed. Hence, the ﬁre strategy cannot conclude
a detailed analysis. Thus, there will not be made any calculations regarding the
ﬁre safety strategy of House 1.
==PAGE:126==
114
Fire strategy
However, an example of diﬀerent ﬂoor plan solutions have been drafted together
with a design solution of a superstructure. This will be presented in the next
chapter. On the basis of this example a short analysis of a possible ﬁre strategy,
will now be presented.
The renovated building is a rectangular building with six ﬂoors, a total height of
approximately 20 meters, and a height of about 16.5 meters to the ﬂoor in the
top ﬂoor. The design of the building is created to comply with the guidelines
for prescriptive based ﬁre safety. The access to the building is through a gallery
with three staircases and two elevators, as seen in Figure 6.1.
(a) Illustration of the front of House 1 with the superstructure
(b) Illustration of the bag of House 1 with the superstructure
Figure 6.1: House 1:
Drawings of the building after the superstructure is
added, clariﬁes the placement of the staircases, elevators and bal-
conies.
6.1.1
Fire Safety pre-ﬂashover
The aim of the pre-ﬂashover ﬁre strategy is to guarantee the safety of the people
in case of a ﬁre incident. The whole building has one usage category, which is
Category 4. This is because the building is used at night for sleeping, and that
the residents have knowledge upon location and utilization of the ﬁre exits.
==PAGE:127==
6.1 Fire analysis of House 1
115
There is no increased risk of ﬁre, as the height to the upper ﬂoor is less than 22
meters. Therefore, the buildings ﬁre strategy can be based on the "Eksempel-
samlingen" [oB06]. However, if you look at the ﬁre load, of the building, it is like
any other residential building. There are many kitchens that can increase the
risk of ﬁre, as well as a lot of ﬂammable materials in form of clothes, furniture,
etc.
The requirements for the building is determined in a combination of the Building
regulations of 2015 [BR115], SBI guidelines [Aag08] and "Eksempelsamlingen"
[oB06]. Due to the fact, that the building is designed as prescriptive based and
therefore works as a traditional construction in relation to ﬁre. To fulﬁll this
some overall guidelines must be met:
• There is two independent ﬁre exits. In this case the gallery and the bal-
conies are available for the lower ﬂoors, while the top ﬂoors has rescue
openings.
• There is no ﬁre section above 600 m2.
• There is no room/ ﬁre compartment over 150 m2.
• There is under 25 meters to the nearest escape route.
• Escape routes is at least 1.3 meters wide.
• Doors are at least 0.77 meters wide in the ﬁre compartments and 1.2
meters for the staircases.
• Doors combined to the escape routes opens in direction of the escape.
• Each ﬁre unit/compartment has a rescue opening.
• Each ﬁre unit must have at minimum one smoke alarm, that are connected
to the power supply and with backup battery..
The overall ﬁre strategy is a complete evacuation. Taking place through the
gallery and the staircases. The elevators will be dimension, so that they can be
used for evacuation of eg. disabled residents. Furthermore, they will be used
as a service elevator for the rescue service. The elevators will have a secured
power supply. After the evacuation is completed, the meeting point will be on
the south/ west side of the building on the parking lot.
An example of ﬁre sectioning of the building is illustrated on Figure 6.2, for scale
see Prospectus 6.1. The blue color indicates ﬁre compartments, red visualizes
ﬁre sections.
Each ﬁre section must fulﬁll REI120 A2-s1-d0 building parts,
==PAGE:128==
116
Fire strategy
except on the top ﬂoor where REI60 is suﬃcient. In this case it concerns the
gallery, elevators, vertical ﬁre walls, and horizontal ﬁre sections. Furthermore,
the ﬁre components are divided by at least EI60 building parts.
Beside the
ﬁre sectioning there is some other passive ﬁre initiatives. The elevators will be
pressurized, this is not a necessity for the staircases, as they are placed in open
air.
It can be seen on Figure 6.2 that there is at most 13 meters to the exit of a
one-plan apartment and 24 meters for a two-plan apartment. The rest of the
evacuation to the ground goes through the three staircases and if necessary the
two elevators connected to the gallery. Each apartment has one rescue opening,
which is either a balcony or a window on the south side of the building. Fur-
thermore, the ﬁgure shows that each apartment function as a ﬁre compartment
and each two apartments have vertical ﬁre section separations, together with
the basement division and the four ﬂoor divisions.
(a) Vertical ﬁre separations of House 1 and marks of escape routes
(b) Horizontal ﬁre separations of House 1
Figure 6.2: House 1: Fire section and ﬁre compartment divisions together with
location of exit routes and rescue openings.
==PAGE:129==
6.1 Fire analysis of House 1
117
The building is placed in connection with another building, therefor there is a
risk of ﬁre spreading between the two buildings. In case of renovation, it is thus
important to ensure ﬁre spreading between the building parts. Besides that one
building, there is a great distance to the surrounding buildings and they are
therefor not a high risk for ﬁre spreading.
Existing conditions contains access roads on three sides of the building.
In
relation to the renovation, there will be established a road on the south side of
the building, such that the balconies and rescue openings can be accessed, by
the rescue service. The road will be at minimum 2.8 meters wide so it can be
access by the rescue services lifts, see Figure 6.3.
Figure 6.3: House 1: The red lines marks the existing roads and the blue
line marks the road that will be conducted in connection with the
renovation to make access for the rescue service on each side of
the building.
6.1.2
Fire Safety post-ﬂashover, Structural ﬁre Safety
The post-ﬂashover ﬁre safety regards the structural ﬁre safety.
As there is
not taken any choice regarding the building parts of the superstructure, the
structural ﬁre safety will not be investigated. Therefore, only the structural ﬁre
safety of the existing construction will be presented.
Horizontal ﬁre division
The existing horizontal ﬁre section divisions consists of Bauma 15cm
and reinforced concrete 10cm, which fulﬁlls the ﬁre requirements,
from the building time which was BS60. By using the conversion
==PAGE:130==
118
Fire strategy
from DBI [DBI08] BS60 fulﬁlls modern requirements for REI60 A2-
s1,d0 building parts. However, the requirement after the superstruc-
ture is added is REI120 A2-s1,d0. Therefor the existing horizontal
construction most be wrapped in ﬁre protection, if they should work
as ﬁre section divisions. This could be done with PAROC FPS plates
[Par17], which can be connected to the existing deck, or any other
ﬁre protection.
Fire walls
The ﬁre walls consists of 3/4 wide brick stones spanning the entire
height. At the building time they fulﬁlled the requirements of BS60
for ﬁre section walls. However, after the superstructure is added the
walls most fulﬁll the requirements for REI120 A2-s1,d0. According
"Murefagets oplysningsråd" a massive masonry wall with a thickness
of 170mm can withstand ﬁre in 120 minutes, which relates to a 3/4
brick.
The covering must be checked to see if it fulﬁlls A2-s1,d0
otherwise it must be rectiﬁed.
Apartment partition walls
The ﬂat division walls also consists of 3/4 wide brick stones. But un-
like the ﬁre walls, they function as separation of the ﬁre cells instead
of the ﬁre sections. Therefore, the walls must fulﬁll EI60 require-
ment, however as they already satisﬁes REI120, this requirement is
fulﬁlled with no further investigation.
The diﬀerent ﬁre divisions have been presented. As described the ﬁre cell di-
visions fulﬁlls today’s requirements, however the ﬁre section division does not
in all cases. As seen in the previous section on Figure 6.2, it is chosen not to
use the existing ﬂoor divisions as ﬁre section. Since it requires an extensive
intervention compared to the ﬁre walls, in order to upgrade the ﬁre safety spec-
tra. Both horizontal and vertical divisions are not necessary to remain under
the requirement of maximum 600 m2 per section. The chosen solution to make
the existing construction fulﬁll modern structural ﬁre regulations, is therefor to
reinforce the basement deck for ﬁre, along with checking the covering of the ﬁre
walls, and update them if necessary.
The ﬁre section separation for the superstructure can be performed in several
diﬀerent ways. However the ﬁre sections must never exceed 600 m2. As the
existing ﬁre sections is almost 600 m2, there must be a full ﬁre division between
the two building parts. Furthermore, the ﬁre cells must not exceed 150m2.

6.2 Fire analysis of House 2 and House 3

6.2
Fire analysis of House 2 and House 3
The ﬁre safety for House 2 and House 3 will be presented together. This is
because a major part of the ﬁre safety is identical within the two cases, hence
repetition is avoided.
6.2.1
Description of the buildings
House 2 has a total height of approximately 21 meters, and an eﬀective height
of about 17.5 meters to the ﬂoor on the top ﬂoor. House 3 has a total height
of approximately 20 meters, and an eﬀective height to the top ﬂoor at about 17
meters. Both buildings are designed as rectangles with six ﬂoors. In both cases,
the existing building contains the three lower ﬂoors, and the new superstructure
contains the three upper ﬂoors. The superstructure for House 2 has the same
design on each ﬂoor, whereas House 3 varies at each ﬂoor.

(b) Illustration of the bag of House 2 with the superstructure

Access to House 2 is provided through the existing staircases on the lower three
ﬂoors. From there, each third staircase is continued up to the new upper ﬂoors
along with new elevators. That gives three staircases and three elevators to the
upper ﬂoors, connected with a gallery to the apartments see Figure 6.4. For
House 3 all of the existing staircases are continued up to the new levels, and a
elevator is connected to each staircase inside the building, see Figure 6.5. An
emergency plan for the two buildings, where the procedure for evacuation and
emergency call are shown, can be seen in Appendix 6.1 and 6.2, this plan will
be located on ground ﬂoor in each staircase in both buildings.
The superstructures are designed such that they fulﬁll the requirements for
prescriptive based ﬁre safety according to the "Eksempel samling" [oB06] and
Building regulations [BR115]. However, the ﬁre safety will be checked for perfor-
mance based scenarios to ensure satisfying ﬁre protection for the entire building,
regarding the existing parts and the superstructures. As described in Section
4.3, the ﬁre safety of the existing building part and the new building part are
related. Therefore, the buildings are treated as one connected building in these
==PAGE:133==
6.2 Fire analysis of House 2 and House 3

sections, and not as an existing and new building part.
6.2.2
Fire Safety pre-ﬂashover
As mentioned in the overall introduction the aim of this part, is to guarantee
the safety of the people in case of a ﬁre incident. This is done by setting up a
ﬁre safety strategy for the buildings. The performance based check is made for
three diﬀerent ﬁre scenarios per house.
6.2.2.1
Principles for ﬁre safety
The overall principles of the ﬁre safety will be listed along with corresponding
drawings and assumptions.
Categories of usage
The buildings have the same usage category, which is Category 4. The category
is provided on, that the buildings are used doing night for sleeping purposes,
and that the residents have knowledge upon location and utilization of the ﬁre
exits. It is assumed that all people in the building are in position to reach safety
by themselves.
Risks and ﬁre load
The buildings and their usage are not expected to present a greater risk of ﬁre
than other buildings with the same usage, as both buildings are less than 22
meters high. Characteristic for buildings in Category 4 is that they are used for
living purpose in one form or another, in this case apartments. Which entails
a certain proportion of kitchens in the properties, increasing the risk of ﬁre.
The ﬁre load in apartments are relatively high. Which comes from furniture,
electrical appliances, clothing and other ﬂammable materials.
Reasons for the ﬁre technical dimensioning
The superstructures can be deﬁned as prescriptive based traditional construc-
tions, while they fulﬁll requirements determined by the Building regulations of
2015 [BR115], SBI guidelines [Aag08] and the "Eksempelsamlingen" [oB06].
• There are at minimum one staircase for escape and one rescue opening or
balcony. 1
1It is okay that there is only one staircase according to the "Eksempelsamling" [oB06]
chapter 2
==PAGE:134==
122
Fire strategy
• Each ﬁre section is maximum 600 m2.
• There is no room/ﬁre compartment over 150 m2.
• There is under 25 metes to the nearest escape route.
• Escape routes are at least 1.3 meters wide.
• Doors are at least 0.77 meters wide in the ﬁre compartments, and 1.2
meters for the staircases. All doors opens in the direction of escape.
• At minimum one rescue opening in each apartment, while there are under
10 persons per apartment.
The performance based check of the buildings cannot be assimilated as a tradi-
tional construction, therefor the ﬁre safety will be documented by "Information
om brandteknisk dimensionering" [oB04].
Fire strategy
The overall ﬁre strategy is a complete evacuation to terrain in the open. Within
House 2 it will take place through the staircase for the three lower ﬂoors, and
the gallery and staircases for the three upper ﬂoors. The evacuation of House
3 will take place through the staircases for all six ﬂoors.
The elevators will
function as service elevators for the rescue service and evacuation of disabled
residents within House 2. However, the elevator are designed for evacuation if it
becomes necessary in the case, that a staircase is blocked, since there is only one
stairway for escape for House 2. However, in House 3 the elevator is connected
to the staircase and can therefore not comply as a ﬁre section by it self. It will
therefor be shot down in case of ﬁre.
Fire protection
The ﬁre protection will as described earlier be based on the building regulations
2015 [BR115], "Eksempelsamling" [oB06] and "Information om brandteknisk
dimensionering" [oB04]. The ﬁre installations will provide a basis for the level
of safety.
BR15: 5.1 pcs. 1 to 2
– Satisfying security against ﬁre and ﬁre spread to other buildings.
– Safe opportunity for rescuing of people and for extinguishing work.
– Fire safety must be maintained throughout the life of the building.
BR15: 5.2 pcs. 1 to 7
==PAGE:135==
6.2 Fire analysis of House 2 and House 3
123
– Evacuation to the ground in the open, using escape routes that are
easy to identify, reach and use.
– Wall-, ceiling- and ﬂoor-coverings in escape routes must not con-
tribute to ﬁre and smoke development during the evacuation period.
– Each ﬁre device must have a rescue opening.
BR15: 5.3 pcs. 1 to 3
– Building parts must be assembled so that the overall construction is
not inferior than the individual part and to avoid spreading of ﬁre.
BR15: 5.4 pcs. 1+2+13
– Fire installations must be checked and maintained throughout their
lifetime.
– Each apartment must be equipped with smoke alarm system, con-
nected to the power supply and with battery backup. Multi-storey
apartments must have at least one alarm per ﬂoor.
BR15: 5.5 pcs. 1
– Spread ﬁre to other ﬁre devices must be prevented during the evac-
uation time.
BR15: 5.6 pcs. 1
– The rescue service must have proper conditions.
Evacuation
In the case of ﬁre there will be a total evacuation.
Although the staircases
is regarded as a safe place, meaning that the residents achieves safety when
reaching the staircases.
Besides the staircases, there will be a rescue opening in each apartment and in
multi-storey apartments one on each ﬂoor. Which can be used for evacuation
by the rescue service if necessary. The evacuation will be as described above.
When the evacuation is completed, the meeting point will be on the parking
lot north of the building for House 2 and in the playground area north of the
building for House 3.

6.2.2.2
Location of the building on the site
Both House 2 and House 3 is a part of a group of buildings placed on one large
site, thus there is a risk of ﬁre spreading to the surrounding buildings, which
must be investigated.
During the renovation, the existing facades can be refurbished, if desired. But
the existing facades can also be preserved. The new facades from Cembrit are
==PAGE:138==
126
Fire strategy
of non-combustible material, A2, s1-d0 Appendix 5.1. To assess the necessary
distances for surrounding buildings as well as the common boundary, account
must be taken of the most critical facade cladding, which are the existing facades.
As no description of their ﬁre properties is within the archive material, they are
considered to be inferior to facade covering than Class 1, K1 10 B-s1,d0. This
gives the following minimum distances for location of the property;
• 5 meters to common boundary, road, or path.
• 10 meters to other buildings.
House 2 is located along with four other buildings. Four of the buildings are
located in a square, giving some critical distance at the corners. Here the min-
imum distance is about 6 meters. Therefore, it must be checked whether the
existing facades comply with K1 10 B-s1,d0. If this is the case, a distance down
to 5 meters is approved. If not, the existing facades must be refurbished to-
gether with the superstructure, or an application for dispensation for a smaller
distance than 10 meters must be made. The location of the properties is shown
in Figure 6.8

shortest distance the the neighbor building as 6 meters.

meters as marked in Figure 6.9 where the site plan for House 3 is illustrated.

The time needed for the smoke layer to reach the criteria is checked with regards
to the evacuation time. The smoke layer increases diﬀerent in the ﬁrst phase
of ﬁre, the growing phase, within the second phase the smoke formation is
conceived as steady. However doing this study, it is assumed that the ﬁre in all
cases are steady, since this gives the most conservative calculations.
==PAGE:141==
6.2 Fire analysis of House 2 and House 3
129
6.2.2.4
Fire safety design
Dimension strategy
The two buildings are dimensioned for performance based conditions. The di-
mension strategy is to look at three diﬀerent cases of ﬁre for each building:
House 2:
– Scenario 1: If ﬁre blocks the middle staircase.
– Scenario 2: Fire in a kitchen.
– Scenario 3: Fire in multistory apartment, kitchen.
House 3:
– Scenario 1: Total evacuation.
– Scenario 2: Fire in kitchen.
– Scenario 3: Fire in multistory apartment, kitchen.
The scenarios are determined on the background of illuminating the following
cases: illustrating quick smoke ﬁlling, blocking one evacuation route, and hidden
propagation. The burning materiel is based on the furniture, machines, and
decorations in the building. All the scenarios has a realistic chance to happen,
while all the burning part is easy to ignite.
Physical description of the design ﬁre
The furniture of the buildings may mostly be ﬂammable materials, thus the ﬁre
cases are assumed to spread by an average speed. Therefore, the growth rate
for all cases, for both houses, are set to medium. This gives a α-value equal to
0.012, from table 4.1.1 in Formula ﬁnder [Ben14]. The occupant densities are set
to 0.05 persons per square meter for both houses. Which gives 5 persons for each
100 m2. For each scenario the item causing the ﬁre is determined together with
its heat release rate, which is based on experiments from [Swe93] and [Spe08]:
House 2:
– Scenario 1: Only evacuation time, so not important.
– Scenario 2: Coﬀee maker, Q = 40kW, polystyrene.
– Scenario 3: Coﬀee maker, Q = 40kW, polystyrene.
House 3:
==PAGE:142==
130
Fire strategy
– Scenario 1: Only evacuation time, so not important.
– Scenario 2: Coﬀee maker, Q = 40kW, polystyrene.
– Scenario 3: Coﬀee maker, Q = 40kW, polystyrene.
Critical position of the ﬁre
The placement of the ﬁres in the ﬁre scenarios are based on the longest dis-
tance to the escape route, and when eﬀecting most people. The scenarios are
illustrated on drawing 6.4 and 6.5 in the Prospectus with corresponding expla-
nations. As an example, scenario 2 for House 2 is illustrated on Figure 6.10.
Figure 6.10: House 2: Illustration of ﬁre scenario 2.
Dimensioning (Performance based)
The scenarios have been calculated and the results have been analyzed. The
input values and calculations are in Appendix 6.3 and 6.4 for House 2 and 3
respectively. Below the results of the diﬀerent cases are listed in Table 6.1 and

The reason why scenario 1 is only checked for total evacuation time, is because
it is only a check to see if a suﬃcient number of staircases have been established.
It is seen for both houses that the total evacuation, to the outside, is satisfying.
The main part of the evacuation period is caused by queue formation at the exit
door. In House 2 the evacuation consists of about four times as many people per
staircase than for that of House 3. The queue formation is therefore greater for
House 2, and causes a bigger total evacuation time, even-though the evacuation
distance is almost the same for both buildings.
Scenario 2 for both houses deals with a kitchen based ﬁre in a one-leveled apart-
ment. It is seen that the evacuation time out of the apartment is completed
before critical conditions arise for both houses.
The reason for disregarding
pre-movement time is due to the fact that, the scenario deals exclusively with
the accident apartment, and it is expected that people within the apartment
react immediately in the event of a ﬁre. Therefore, the calculation does not
take people, in the aﬀected apartment, that do not respond to the ﬁre alarm
or visible ﬁre into account. The safety for people that do not react cannot be
==PAGE:144==
132
Fire strategy
fulﬁlled no matter which ﬁre measures that are conducted in the building.
Scenario 3 deals with ﬁre in the kitchen, similar to scenario 2, but in this case
in a two-level apartment. It is seen that the evacuation is not completed before
critical conditions arise, when an exit is only existing in the bottom ﬂoor. This
is solved by establishing exit on both ﬂoors of the apartments. In this way, the
evacuation time becomes similar to that in scenario 2, thus it can be completed
before critical conditions occur.
6.2.2.5
Passive ﬁre safety
The passive ﬁre safety is carried out together with the establishment of the
building. Passive ﬁre safety is active all the time, if there is a ﬁre or not.
Fire sections
The ﬁre sections are carried out using REI120 A2-s1,d0 building parts on all
ﬂoors, except for the top ﬂoor where REI60 A2-s1,d0 is acceptable. The ﬁre
compartments/cells are divided by EI60 building parts. Furthermore, doors to
ﬁre sections are at least EI60. The sectioning of the two buildings can be seen in
the Prospectus 6.2 and 6.3. The staircases must be conducted using minimum
R30 A2-s1,d0 building parts.
Other passive ﬁre safety precautions
Besides the ﬁre sections and compartments, there is some other passive ﬁre
initiatives for both buildings listen below:
• Pressurized staircases.
• Pressurized elevators.
• Signs and marks for evacuation on ground ﬂoor.
• The doors to the staircases and exit doors will be 1.5 meters wide and the
apartment doors 1.2 meter.
6.2.2.6
Active ﬁre safety
The active ﬁre safety is the safety that are activated in case of a ﬁre. This can
be diﬀerent through the building on each ﬂoor.
==PAGE:145==
6.2 Fire analysis of House 2 and House 3
133
Active ﬁre protection systems
All apartments in both houses are provided with a smoke alarm system, accord-
ing to the Building Regulations 2015.
6.2.2.7
Access of the rescue service
There will be established access roads around the whole building in both cases,
such that the rescue service have access to all exits and rescue openings within 40
meters. The roads will be at minimum 2.8 meters wide. In front of the building
there will be a ﬁre rescue area 4 meters wide, with a distance of 10 meters to the
building. The roads and rescue area for both houses are illustrated on Figure
6.11 and 6.12.
Figure 6.11: House 2: The red lines marks the existing roads and the blue
line marks the road that will be conducted in connection with
the renovation.
So the rescue service has access to all rescue
openings.

rescue service.
In the basement of both buildings, there will be a safe ﬁre service room. This
room will also function as the operation room for the diﬀerent ﬁre systems in
the building, including the elevators.
6.2.2.8
Operation and Maintenance
According to the Building Regulations 2015 [BR115] the ﬁre technical systems
most be performed such that they are reliable and can be easily controlled and
maintained throughout the buildings lifetime.
There will be regular inspections once a year according to DBI recommendations,
[DBI]. These controls will be documented by control schemes, which will be
saved for three years. This includes the elevators.
The caretaker will be responsible for the maintenance and operation of all the
ﬁre systems and other conditions that eﬀects the ﬁre safety. This is in order, such
that there is one to contact always, if concerns arises. It is also the caretakers
responsibility, that all test will be made and documented.
Besides the ﬁre
systems, there are other parts of the ﬁre safety, which most be checked. This
could be, as an example, that the access to the operation room most be clear
==PAGE:147==
6.2 Fire analysis of House 2 and House 3
135
at any time. The evacuation routes and exit doors most never be blocked and
always be easy to reach.
All this will make the operation and maintenance of the ﬁre safety of the building
eﬀective and structured. The necessary documents will be made and the ﬁre
safety will be met, at all time.
6.2.3
Fire Safety post-ﬂashover, Structural ﬁre Safety
The structural ﬁre safety of the buildings will be investigated. This is done by
checking critical elements in both buildings. The check consists of comparing
the reduced bearing capacity after a ﬁre incident, with the reduced accident
load of a ﬁre.
6.2.3.1
Critical elements
The main structure elements in both houses are the bearing inner walls and
slabs. The most critical elements for the existing buildings and the superstruc-
tures are checked for ﬁre accident.
The reduced accidental load, in case of ﬁre, is determined by Equation 6.10:
Ed = Gconstruction + Gother + ψ1.1 · Q + Ad
(6.10)
Where Ad in this case is 0 and the partial coeﬃcient ψ1.1 = 0.3 for this case
[mf.16].
Resistance class
The load bearing elements most have a ﬁre sustainability for at minimum
REI120 and be covered by A2-s1,d0 material.
In the archive material it is
determined, that the cover material of the existing walls in both houses is in-
ﬂammable, which corresponds to A2-s1,d0. For the superstructure ﬁre gypsum
is used as covering material, which fulﬁll the requirements for A2-s1,d0 materi-
als, see data sheet in Appendix 5.3.
The walls that separates the ﬁre compartments/ cells must be separated by
EI60. However these walls are also the bearing walls, so they will be checked as
bearing elements.
==PAGE:148==
136
Fire strategy
Critical elements of the superstructure
In the previous chapter the bearing capacity of all the bearing elements in the
superstructure are checked. However, they need to be checked in case of ﬁre as
well. This is done by checking the reduced bearing capacity in case of ﬁre, with
the accidental load determined by Equation 6.10.
When checking the constructions for ﬁre dimensioning, the conversion factor
for strength is higher. This is because that ﬁre dimensioning allows for a 20%
quantile, where otherwise a 5% quantile is required.
The reduced cross-section is based on the same terms for all the carrying parts
of the super structure, beams as well as columns. It is determined by calcu-
lating the reduced cross-section after ﬁre inﬂuence of 120 minutes. The burned
depth,dchar,n , in case of ﬁre is determined plus 7 mm, which take the weakened
part of the wood into account, according to [mf.16] page 315. When the reduced
cross-sections are determined, the bearing parts are checked as in Chapter 5.
However, the deformation of the bearing beams are not relevant when looking
at the ﬁre dimension. On the other hand, the weak axis must be checked in the
column calculation. This is due to the fact, that the weak axis is not ﬁxed in
case of ﬁre, since the plate material is burned away.
The calculations determines how much ﬁre protection, that are necessary to
fulﬁll the ﬁre requirement of REI120. The ﬁre protection consists of a diﬀerent
number of ﬁre gypsum plates. All the calculation can be found in Appendix 5.7,
5.12, 5.20 and 5.22 for House 2 and Appendix 5.8, 5.13, 5.21 and 5.23 for House
3. In Table 6.3 the ﬁre protection for the diﬀerent constructions can be seen.
In Appendix 5.4 the diﬀerent types of gypsum screws can be seen, depending of
the number of layers. That is, for four layers of ﬁre gypsum, there are used at
minimum 3.9x70mm screws.
Table 6.3: House 2 & 3: List of ﬁre protection used of the wood structures in
the superstructure. Where the most comprehensive is four layers
of ﬁre gypsum.

6.2 Fire analysis of House 2 and House 3
137
Critical elements in the existing building part
The critical wall in House 2 is made by concrete casted on site and concrete
elements in House 3. The critical wall for House 2 is wall ﬁve and in House
3 it is wall one. Both walls are checked for the new increased load from the
superstructure in Chapter 5.
Fire resistance is checked by table values from Eurocode 2.2 DS/EN 1992-1-2
[Sta13a]. The minimum dimension for load-bearing walls is determined by Table
5.4 in [Sta13a] for REI120. In order to be able to read the dimension, the degree
of utilization of the wall must be determined for accidental load. However, since
the utilization is suﬃciently small for ordinary load, this is used as it is on the
safe side. In both cases, µfi < 0.35, which gives a minimum thickness of the
bearing walls of 150 millimeters. The existing walls therefore have suﬃcient ﬁre
resistance to REI120.
It is not enough to check the bearing walls, the slabs must also be checked for
ﬁre. The ﬁre resistance is corresponding to the walls checked by table values
from Eurocode 2.2 [Sta13a]. The minimum thickness is determined by Table
5.8 for REI120 to be 120 millimeters. Both in House 2 and 3 the existing slabs
is 180 millimeters thick, thereby the ﬁre resistance for 120 minutes is fulﬁlled.
However, this requirement sets a demand for a minimum reinforcement cover
layer of 25 mm. There are no detail drawings, concerning the reinforcements
placement in the deck construction, available in the archive material for both
houses. So the reinforcements placement must be checked.
But the basement deck, in both cases, is only 100 mm thick and it must therefor
be subject to reinforcement regarding ﬁre. The reinforcement can be done in
diﬀerent ways, but one way is to place ﬁre protection in the bottom of the deck.
Fire protection of 30 mm CONLIT 150 Hvid vlies from Rockwool can be used
connected to the existing deck with concrete-adhesive, [Bra].

Fire strategy
==PAGE:151==
Chapter 7
Final concept
In this chapter, the ﬁnal solution for the three superstructures will be presented.
Thus it concludes all of the previous discussions into an actual product.
7.1
Introduction
In the previous sections, the technical description of the superstructures are
described. This section will focus on the design and architectonic parts of the
construction. In relation to this, a 3D model have been prepared for the three
buildings using SketchUp. Likewise, diﬀerent solutions for plan division have
been prepared in AutoCad.
The building regulations ensure accessibility to a certain extent, corresponding
to quality level C [Jen15a]. It is desirable to meet quality level B for the super-
structures, so that they can be used by people with disabilities. This yields some
diﬀerent requirements for the layout of the buildings. Eg. the passage widths
must be at minimum 1.5 meters, where the building regulations only demands
1.0 meter. All the requirements are described in SBI 249 [Jen15a], and they are
fulﬁlled for quality level B in all three cases.
==PAGE:152==
140
Final concept
When the design of the superstructures are to be determined, there is an inﬁnite
sea of possibilities, depending on what the developer wants from the superstruc-
ture. It has been chosen to make three diﬀerent solutions, illustrating three
diﬀerent focus points. This shows that the design of the superstructures are not
limited, even-though it has to be established on existing buildings.
House 1 has no signiﬁcant residual bearing capacity, therefore a major conversion
is necessary to establish a superstructure. It is therefore important, that the
existing residents beneﬁt from the renovation.
Therefore, the focus for the
solution proposal is to increase comfort, as well as providing additional square
meters for the existing residents as well as new residents.
On the other hand, House 2 has high residual capacity. Which opens up for
other opportunities than those of House 1.
The solution proposal has been
prepared, so that the existing residents are disturbed at a minimum. Similarly,
the three new ﬂoors will have the same design. This solution is therefore the
simplest of the three, but provides a high level of utilization of square meters
and a eﬃcent module division.
In House 3 the residual capacity is similar to that of House 2. The solution have
taken the main focus on giving the residents the pleasure of large balconies. This
solution therefore contains displaced plans that create a balcony environment
for the new apartments.
This concludes the main goals of the superstructure design. The goals are visible
from tabel 7.1.
Table 7.1: The main design goals of the three renewal projects are listed below.
Main focus goal of the renewal
House 1
To maximize the entire construction potential.
House 2
To minimize disturbance of the existing residents.
House 3
To create attractive balconies with great sunlight.
==PAGE:153==
7.2 House 1
141
7.2
House 1
The building has no residual capacity for sustaining a superstructure, thus an
intervention in the existing conditions are necessary, in order to establish a su-
perstructure. As previously described, no concrete solution has been developed
for this property, but rather a description of how a superstructure can be made
possible and carried out. When the renovation handles interventions in existing
conditions, the ﬁnal solution must beneﬁt the entire building, including existing
residents. It must therefor introduce some beneﬁts for the residents, so that
they will participate in a renovation. In order to weigh up the genes they are
subjected to, during the reconstruction.
There is no room inside the building optimal to establish an elevator, it was
therefor decided to establish a new gallery with elevators and staircases. The
new entrance to the apartments will take place through the existing bathroom.
The new bathrooms will then be placed in the existing staircase which is abol-
ished. In addition, the kitchens will be renewed with a bay with dining area.
The bays have an added advantage, as they create small private spaces, which
can be used as balconies. The gallery is 4.5 meters deep at the indentation,
which adds extra shade to the room on the north facade. The ﬂoor plans will
be presented in the next section, but due to the shadow eﬀect, the room on the
north side is not ﬁtted as a living room, it will therefor only be used as hallway
or bedroom. Depending on whether internal or external reinforcement of the
existing structures is used, internal or external re-insulation is established.
The superstructure thus contains ﬁve major improvements to the existing con-
ditions, and hence towards the existing residents:
• Elevators
• Added square meters, 15m2 per apartment
• New and bigger bathrooms
• New dining area in the kitchen
• Re-insulation
The overall design of House 1, with a new superstructure is presented by Figure
7.1, where the existing facades are remained.
Additional illustrations of the
building can be found in Prospectus 7.1. Solutions where the existing facade is
preserved and renovated are shown.

7.3
House 2
Contrary to House 1, the desire, in this case, is to aﬀect the existing residents
as little as possible. Therefor, it have been chosen only to extend three of the
existing staircases to the superstructure together with three elevators. With this
solution, only every third entrance will be aﬀected by the renovation. The dis-
advantage to this solution, is that the existing apartments in the other entrances
will not have access to an elevator. However, all apartments in the superstruc-
ture will be connected to the elevators, hence the requirement for an elevator in
buildings greater than three levels are fulﬁlled. The superstructure consists of
modules based on Scandi Byg’s principles, as described in the previous chapters.
However, there are some parts of the structure that is ordered externally; this
concerns the elevators, shaft, staircases, and the gallery construction.

For the superstructure there are two diﬀerent types of apartment designs. In a
size range from 74 m2 to 129 m2 on one or two leveled apartments. In consider-
ation of the water and installation extension to the superstructure, the kitchen
==PAGE:164==
152
Final concept
and bathroom are placed in the same location as in the existing building part.
Where the two-leveled apartments are placed on the 5TH and 6TH ﬂoor. The
apartments for students and disable people are placed on the 4TH ﬂoor of the
building. The number of available apartments after the renovation are summed
up in Table 7.4.
